Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Father Marrying His Son's Slave-Girl: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on validity of father marrying son's slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a father marrying his son's slave-girl, explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
Full ownership cannot be shared; the father's right is only a shubha that drops hadd, not an ownership that prohibits marriage.

This lesson examines a disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the validity of a father marrying his son's slave-girl. The Hanafis permit it, while Shafi'is prohibit it. By tracing each school's proof and rebuttal, you will understand how a key hadith is interpreted differently and what practical consequences follow.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its consequences
The Hanafi madhhab allows a father to marry his son's slave-girl. If a child is born from the father's sperm, the slave-girl does not become umm walad, the father pays the mahr but not the slave-girl's value, and the child is free.
The child is free because the master (the son) is the child's brother, triggering the rule that a close blood relative owned becomes free.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i prohibits such a marriage, arguing from the hadith 'anta wa maluka li-abika' (you and your property belong to your father) that the father has a right of ownership in the son's property. This right, like in the case of intercourse (drop of hadd), should prevent marriage.
The Shafi'i school analogizes the prohibition of marriage from the father's ownership right, similar to the prohibition of marrying one's own slave-girl.
3
Analyse the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis respond that the father's right is not full ownership; it is a weak right (shubha) that only creates doubt sufficient to drop the hadd penalty but does not amount to the complete ownership needed to prohibit marriage.
The Hanafis emphasize that full ownership (milk taam) belongs exclusively to the son, as he can sell, free, or lease the slave-girl.
4
Work through the Hanafi rational proof
The Hanafis also use a rational argument (dalil aqli): it is impossible for two persons to have complete ownership over the same object simultaneously (imtina' al-jam' bayn al-milkayn). Since the son is the absolute owner, the father cannot be.
This argument is based on the principle that ownership is exclusive; a single object cannot be fully owned by two individuals at the same time.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to the Hanafi view, the marriage is valid, the child is free, and the father pays only the mahr. According to the Shafi'i view, the marriage is invalid, potentially rendering the child illegitimate and subject to different rules.
The difference impacts family law, inheritance, and status of offspring in Muslim communities.

باپ دونوں کا مالک نہیں ہے۔ البتہ باپ پر مہر لازم ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس نے نکاح کے ذریعہ مبر کا التزام کیا ہے۔ اور اس باندی سے باپ کے نطفہ سے جو بچہ پیدا ہوا ہے وہ آزاد ہو گا اسلئے کہ باندی کا بچہ مولیٰ کی ملک ہوا کرتا ہے۔ اور یہاں مولی اس بچہ کا بھائی ہے۔ لہذا قرابت کی وجہ سے یہ بچہ اپنے بھائی (مولی ) پر آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ حدیث من ملک ذکی رحم محرم معتق علیہ کی وجہ سے۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary on Hidayah, Volume 4, Book of Nikah: Due to the commitment made through marriage. And that slave-girl's child will be free. Because her brother is her owner. Therefore, due to kinship, he became free. Explanation: The case is that a son married his slave-girl to his father, then a child was born from the father's sperm from that slave-girl. Then that slave-girl will not be the father's umm walad, nor will the father owe the value of the slave-girl to the son. However, the father must pay the mahr, and the offspring of that slave-girl from the father's sperm will be free. There is disagreement regarding whether the father's marriage to the son's slave-girl is permissible. The Hanafis hold it permissible, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds it impermissible. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is that the father has a right of ownership in the son's property (due to the hadith 'anta wa maluka li-abika'). This is why if the father had intercourse with his son's slave-girl thinking it is forbidden, still the hadd penalty is not imposed on the father. And any person who has a right of ownership in a slave-girl, his marriage to that slave-girl is not permissible. Therefore, the father's marriage to the son's slave-girl is not permissible. The Hanafis' proof is that the son's slave-girl is completely free of the father's ownership. Consider that the son is the absolute owner of this slave-girl. The proof of absolute ownership is that intercourse with her is lawful for the son, and if he frees her, the freeing takes effect. When the son is absolute owner, it is impossible for the father to also be absolute owner, because combining two ownerships for two persons in a single object at a single time is impossible. Second reason: the son has the right to make such dispositions in this slave-girl that, even if the father had ownership, it would not remain. For example, the son can sell, gift, free, or lease this slave-girl. This indicates the absence of the father's ownership. 'But the hadd is dropped...' is the response to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof. The summary of the response is that the hadd is dropped from the father due to the suspicion of ownership, acting upon the apparent meaning of the hadith 'anta wa maluka li-abika'. In any case, when the son's slave-girl is free from the father's ownership, the father's marriage is valid. And when the father's marriage is valid, his semen becomes protected by marriage, so there is no need to establish ownership by right of possession for the father. And when ownership by right of possession is not established for the father in the son's slave-girl, this slave-girl will not be the father's umm walad. The father will not owe the value of the slave-girl nor of her children, because the father owns neither of them. However, the father must pay the mahr because he committed to it through marriage. And the child born from the father's sperm from this slave-girl will be free because the slave-girl's child is the property of the master. And here the master is the child's brother. Therefore, due to kinship, this child becomes free upon his brother (the master) based on the hadith 'man malaka dha rahim mahramin mu'taqun alayh' (whoever owns a close blood relative, that relative is free upon him).
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of a father marrying his son's slave-girl. The Hanafi school permits it, while Imam al-Shafi'i prohibits it. The author first states the ruling: if a son marries his slave-girl to his father and a child is born, the slave-girl does not become an umm walad for the father, the father owes no compensation to the son for the slave-girl, but the father must pay the mahr, and the child is free. Then the dispute is explained. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the father has a right of ownership in his son's property based on the hadith 'anta wa maluka li-abika' (you and your property belong to your father). This right of ownership prevents marriage, just as it drops the hadd penalty for intercourse under suspicion. The Hanafis respond that the father's ownership is not absolute; the son is the complete owner. Ownership cannot be shared in a single object completely. The father's right is only a weak right that prevents hadd but does not establish full ownership necessary to prohibit marriage. Since the father has no ownership right in the slave-girl, the marriage is valid. Consequently, the father's semen is protected by marriage, not by ownership, so no umm walad status arises. The child is free because the child's master is the son (the child's brother), and the hadith on freeing close relatives applies.

Key Terms
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who bears a child from her master; she becomes free upon the master's death and cannot be sold.
ملک یمین (milk yamin): Right of possession by ownership, as opposed to milk nikah (ownership by marriage).
انت ومالک لابیک (anta wa maluka li-abika): Hadith meaning 'You and your property belong to your father', indicating a special right of the father over the son's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a son marries his slave-girl to his father and a child is born, what is the status of the child?
A) The child is a slave of the father.
B) The child is free because the master is the child's brother.
C) The child is a slave of the son.
D) The child is free because the father owns him.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a father to marry his son's slave-girl because the father does not have full ownership; the son's absolute ownership prevents any complete ownership by the father.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Free Woman Acquires Her Slave Husband: Implied Sale Invalidates Marriage
Intra-Hanafi dispute with proof and counter-proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi majority position on the invalidity of marriage when a free woman acquires her slave husband through an implied sale, and contrast it with Imam Zufar's view.

Core Idea
A free woman cannot marry her own slave; an implied sale that transfers ownership invalidates the marriage.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about a free woman married to a slave who asks the master to free him for a price. The majority holds the marriage invalid due to conflict of ownership, while Imam Zufar argues the freeing cannot transfer ownership since the woman never owned the slave. This lesson unpacks the proofs and the principle of iqtida' (implication) used by the majority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
A free woman is married to a slave husband. She asks his master: 'Free him for me for a thousand dirhams.' The master does so. What happens to the marriage? The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) say the marriage becomes invalid (fasid). Imam Zufar says it remains valid.
The ruling depends on whose behalf the freeing takes effect.
2
Understand Imam Zufar's proof
Zufar argues that a person cannot free what he does not own. The woman does not own the slave, so requesting the master to free him on her behalf is impossible. Therefore the freeing takes effect on behalf of the master, not the woman. Ownership does not transfer, so the marriage remains valid.
Zufar's reasoning is based on literal impossibility: 'la itq fi ma la yamlikuhu ibn Adam.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi majority's response
The majority responds by using iqtida' (implication). To give effect to the woman's words and avoid futility, we imply a prior sale: her request 'Free him for me for a thousand' is interpreted as 'Sell him to me for a thousand, then free him on my behalf.' The master's acceptance implies sale. Thus the woman becomes owner, and then the freeing is valid on her behalf. Once she owns her husband, the marriage becomes invalid due to conflict between milk al-nikah and milk al-yamin.
This is a classic application of the usul principle: 'al-qawl yuhmalu 'ala al-sihhah' (speech is interpreted in a way that makes it effective).

طلب کرنا صحیح نہیں ہوا۔ پس آزادی مامور کی طرف سے واقع ہو جائے گی اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ آمر کے ( کلام ) طلب اعتماق کو صحیح کرناممکن ہے اقتضاء ملک کو مقدم مان کر اسلئے کہ آمر کی طرف سے آزادی صحیح ہونے فاسد ہوگیا اور امام زفر کے نزدیک فاسد نہیں ہوگا۔ اختلاف کی بنیاد یہ ہے کہ ہمارے نزدیک آزادی آمریعنی حرۃ کی طرف سے واقع ہوئی ہے حتی کہ ولاء آمر ہی کیلئے ہوگا۔ اور اگر آمر نے اس آزادی سے کفارہ کی نیت کر لی تو آمر کی طرف سے کفارہ ادا ہو جائے گا۔ اور آمر کفارہ کی ذمہ داری سے سبکدوش ہو جائے گا۔ اور امام زفر کے نزدیک آزادی مامور (مولی ) کی طرف سے واقع ہوگی۔ امام زفر کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ آمریعنی حرہ نے اپنے شوہر کے مولیٰ سے اس بات کی درخواست کی کہ وہ اپنا غلام اس کی طرف سے آزاد کر دے۔ اور یہ امر محال ہے کیونکہ آدمی جس کا مالک نہیں ہوتا اس میں اس کی طرف سے آزادی واقع نہیں ہو سکتی ہے۔ لہذا آمریعنی حرہ کا آزادی کی درخواست کرناہی صحیح نہیں ہے۔ پس غلام کی یہ آزادی مامور کی طرف سے واقع ہوگی نہ کہ آمر کی طرف سے۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ آمر کے کلام کو لغو ہونے سے بچانے کیلئے اس کو صحیح کرنے کی کوشش کی جائے گی ۔ درانحالیکہ آمر کے کلام کو صحیح کرنا ممکن بھی ہے بایں طور کہ اقتضاء آمر کیلئے ملک کو متقدم مان لیا جائے گا اسلئے کہ صحت عشق کیلئے ملک شرط ہے اب آمریعنی حرہ کے قول اعتقه عنی بالف کی تقدیری عبارت ہوگی بعده عنى بالف ثم كن وكيلى بالاعتاق یعنی میرے ہاتھ اس غلام کو ایک ہزار کے عوض فروخت کر دے پھر میری طرف سے وکیل ہو کر اس کو آزاد کر دے جواب میں مولیٰ کے قول اعقت کی تقدیری عبارت ہوگی بعتک واعقته عنگ یعنی میں نے تجھ کوفروخت کیا اور تیری طرف سے اس کو آزاد کر دیا۔ پس جب اقتضاء بیع پائی گئی تو آمریعنی حرہ کیلئے ملک
He said: And if a free woman is married to a slave, and she said to his master: 'Free him for me for a thousand,' and he did so, then the marriage becomes invalid. And Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It does not become invalid. The basis is that according to us, the freeing takes effect on behalf of the orderer (the woman), so that the wala' belongs to her, and if he intended expiation with it, he exits from its obligation. According to him (Zufar), it takes effect on behalf of the one ordered (the master), because she requested that the master free his slave on her behalf, and this is impossible because there is no freeing of what one does not own. So the request is not valid, and the freeing takes effect on behalf of the master. And our proof is that it is possible to correct it by advancing ownership by way of implication, because ownership is a condition for the validity of freeing on behalf of someone. So her statement 'Free him for me for a thousand' becomes a request for ownership from him for a thousand, then a command to free her slave for her, and his statement 'I freed' becomes a granting of ownership to her and then freeing on her behalf. And when ownership is established for the orderer, the marriage becomes invalid due to the conflict between the two ownerships (ownership by marriage and ownership by right of possession). Translation: And if a free woman is married to a slave, then she said to the slave's master: 'Free him for me for a thousand,' and the master did so, then the marriage becomes invalid. Imam Zufar said it does not become invalid. The basis of the difference is that according to us, the freeing takes effect on behalf of the orderer, so that the wala' belongs to her, and if she intended expiation with it, she exits from its obligation. According to Imam Zufar, it takes effect on behalf of the one ordered (the master), because she requested that the master free his slave on her behalf, and this is impossible because a person cannot free what he does not own. Therefore the request is not valid, and the freeing takes effect on behalf of the master. Our proof is that it is possible to correct her speech by implying ownership beforehand, because ownership is a condition for the validity of freeing on behalf of someone. So her statement 'Free him for me for a thousand' becomes a request for ownership from him for a thousand, then a command to free her slave for her, and the master's statement 'I freed' becomes a granting of ownership to her and then freeing on her behalf. And when ownership is established for the orderer, the marriage becomes invalid due to the conflict between two ownerships (ownership by marriage and ownership by right of possession). Explanation: The case is that a free woman is married to a slave. She said to her husband's master: 'Free this slave for me for a thousand dirhams.' The master said, 'I have freed him.' In this case, according to the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), the marriage becomes invalid, while Imam Zufar says it does not become invalid. The basis of the difference is that according to us, the freeing takes effect on behalf of the orderer (the free woman), so that the wala' belongs to her. And if she intended expiation with this freeing, then the expiation is fulfilled on her behalf. According to Imam Zufar, the freeing takes effect on behalf of the master. Imam Zufar's proof is that the free woman requested the master to free his slave on her behalf, and this is impossible because a person cannot free what he does not own. Therefore her request itself is not valid, so the freeing takes effect on behalf of the master, not the woman. Our proof is that we try to correct her speech to avoid it being futile, and it is possible to correct it by implying ownership for the woman beforehand, because ownership is a condition for the validity of the freeing on her behalf. Now, the free woman's words 'Free him for me for a thousand' have an implied meaning: 'Sell him to me for a thousand, then be my agent in freeing him.' In response, the master's words 'I freed' have the implied meaning: 'I sold him to you and freed him on your behalf.' So when the sale by implication is established, ownership is established for the free woman, and then the marriage becomes invalid due to the conflict between the two ownerships (marriage ownership and ownership by right of possession).
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a free woman married to a slave. The woman asks the slave's master to free him for her in exchange for a thousand dirhams. The majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that the marriage becomes invalid upon the master's acceptance, whereas Imam Zufar maintains the marriage remains valid. The core of the dispute lies in whose behalf the freeing takes effect. The Hanafi majority argue that the woman's request is to be interpreted as a request to buy the slave and then have him freed on her behalf. This is possible by implying a sale first; thus ownership of the slave transfers to the woman, and then the master frees him on her behalf. Once the woman owns the slave (her husband), the marriage becomes invalid because a woman cannot be married to her own slave (conflict between milk al-nikah and milk al-yamin). Imam Zufar, however, argues that freeing on behalf of someone who does not own the slave is impossible, so the request is void and the freeing remains with the master; thus ownership does not transfer, and the marriage continues. The Hanafi majority's position is based on the principle of giving effect to the speech (ta'sir al-qawl) by implication, avoiding futility ('adam al-ilgha').

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): The bond of loyalty and inheritance that arises between the liberator and the freed slave.
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Implication; a legal interpretation where a statement is understood to include what is necessary for its validity.
ملک النکاح (milk al-nikah): The right of ownership in marriage, i.e., the husband's right to his wife and vice versa within marital bounds.
ملک الیمین (milk al-yamin): Ownership by right of possession, i.e., ownership of a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), why does the marriage become invalid when a free woman asks the master to free her slave husband for a price?
A) Because the master's freeing is invalid.
B) Because the woman acquires ownership of her husband through an implied sale, conflicting with marriage.
C) Because the woman divorces her husband.
D) Because the husband becomes free and no longer needs the marriage.

Key Takeaway
When a free woman asks her slave husband's master to free him for a price, the Hanafi majority holds the marriage invalid due to an implied sale that transfers ownership to her, creating a conflict between marriage and slave ownership.

Nikah — Marriage

Freedom for a Price: The Marriage Invalidity Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage validity due to implied ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on marriage invalidity in the scenario of a free woman freeing her slave-husband through the master, and explain the reasoning behind Imam Zufar's dissent.

Core Idea
A wife cannot own her husband; implied ownership invalidates the marriage.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi debate about whether a marriage becomes invalid when a free woman, married to a slave, asks the slave's master to free him on her behalf for a price. The majority of Hanafi imams hold that the marriage is invalidated, while Imam Zufar disagrees. Understanding the legal reasoning behind each position deepens your grasp of the interplay between ownership, emancipation, and marital capacity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the marriage's validity after the woman's request and manumission.
The marriage becomes invalid (fasid).
2
Identify Imam Zufar's opposing view
State Imam Zufar's ruling and the core reason for his disagreement.
He holds the marriage remains valid because emancipation occurs on the master's behalf.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
Examine Imam Zufar's argument that the woman cannot free what she does not own, so the act is attributed to the master.
This is based on the impossibility of freeing a slave one does not own.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how the majority uses istihsan and the principle of iqtida' (implication) to validate the woman's words and establish her ownership.
They imply a prior sale and agency to avoid futility in her speech.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence of the dispute: whether the marriage remains valid or becomes invalid, affecting the couple's legal status.
The difference affects inheritance, divorce, and future marriages.

لیکن اس کے باوجود نکاح فاسد نہیں ہو نا چاہتے دو وجہوں سے۔ ایک یہ کہ یہاں آمر کیلئے ملک اقتضاء ثابت ہے اور جو چیز اقتضاء ثابت ہوتی ہے وہ ضروۃ ثابت ہوگی۔ اور جو چیز ضرورۃ ثابت ہوتی ہے وہ بقدر ضرورت ثابت And according to him it occurs on behalf of the commanded because he requested that the commanded free his slave on his behalf, and this is impossible because there is no emancipation of what a son of Adam does not own. So the request is not valid, so the emancipation occurs on behalf of the commanded. And our proof is that it is possible to correct it by presuming ownership in advance by way of implication, since ownership is a condition for the validity of emancipation on his behalf. So his saying 'free on my behalf' becomes a request for transfer of ownership from him with the price, then his command to free the slave of the commander on his behalf, and his saying 'I freed' becomes a transfer of ownership from him then emancipation on his behalf. And when ownership is established for the commander, the marriage becomes invalid due to incompatibility between the two ownerships. Translation: And if a free woman is under a slave (as wife), then that woman said to the slave's master: 'Free him on my behalf in exchange for one thousand,' and the master did so, then the marriage becomes invalid. Imam Zufar said it does not become invalid. The basis of the disagreement is that according to us, emancipation occurs on behalf of the commander (the one giving the command) such that the wala' belongs to him. And if the commander intended expiation with this emancipation, then the commander is relieved of the liability of expiation. According to Imam Zufar, it occurs on behalf of the commanded (the one commanded). Because the commander requested that the commanded free his slave on his behalf, and this is impossible because there is no emancipation of what one does not own. Therefore the request is not valid, so emancipation occurs on behalf of the commanded. And our proof is that it is possible to correct the commander's speech by presuming ownership in advance, because ownership is a condition for the validity of emancipation on his behalf. So the commander's saying 'free' means a request for transfer of ownership from the master for one thousand, then a command to free the commander's slave on his behalf, and the master's saying 'I freed' becomes making the commander the owner then freeing on his behalf. And when ownership is established for the commander, the marriage becomes invalid due to incompatibility between the two ownerships (marital ownership and right-hand ownership). Explanation of the case: A free woman is married to a slave. The woman said to the slave's master: 'Free this slave on my behalf in exchange for one thousand dirhams.' The master said: 'I have freed him.' In this case, according to the three imams, the marriage becomes invalid, and according to Imam Zufar, it does not become invalid. The basis of disagreement: According to us, emancipation occurs on behalf of the commander (the free woman), so wala' belongs to her, and if she intended expiation, it is fulfilled. According to Imam Zufar, emancipation occurs on behalf of the commanded (the master). Imam Zufar's proof: The commander (free woman) requested the master to free his slave on her behalf, which is impossible because one cannot free what one does not own. So her request is invalid, so freedom occurs on behalf of the master. Our proof: To prevent the commander's speech from being futile, we try to validate it, and it is possible by presuming ownership for the commander in advance, because ownership is a condition for validity of emancipation. Thus her statement 'free him on my behalf' is interpreted as 'sell him to me for one thousand, then be my agent in freeing him.' The master's response 'I freed' is interpreted as 'I sold him to you and freed him on your behalf.' When the sale by implication is established, ownership is proven for the commander, so marriage becomes invalid due to incompatibility. An objection: If the master explicitly said 'I sold and freed,' emancipation would occur on his behalf by consensus because the commander's acceptance of sale is not present. So when explicit sale does not result in emancipation on her behalf, implied sale should a fortiori not. Answer: Sometimes something is proven implicitly even if not explicitly, like the fetus in the womb is sold as a subsidiary to the mother. A second objection: Even if ownership is proven for the commander, the marriage should not become invalid for two reasons: (1) This ownership is established by implication and is only as needed, which is to validate emancipation, not to extend to invalidating marriage. (2) This ownership vanishes immediately upon the command to free, and such ownership does not invalidate marriage, as when an agent to purchase buys his own wife for his principal, the agent's marriage does not become invalid because ownership vanishes instantly. Answer to first: When something is proven, it is proven with all its consequences, and invalidation of marriage is one consequence. Answer to second: Ownership is established for the principal from the start, not for the agent, so the question does not arise.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect on marriage when a free woman, married to a slave, asks the slave's master to free the slave on her behalf for a specified price. The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the marriage becomes invalid (fasid), while Imam Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl disagrees, stating the marriage remains valid. The crux of the disagreement lies in whose behalf the emancipation is deemed to occur. Imam Zufar reasons that the woman cannot validly request emancipation of a slave she does not own; therefore the emancipation must be attributed to the master (the one commanded). Consequently, no ownership transfers to her and the marriage remains intact. The majority (the three imams) employ the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) to give effect to the woman's words by implying a prior sale. They interpret her statement 'free him on my behalf' as an implicit request for the master to sell the slave to her and then act as her agent in emancipation. The master's response 'I freed' is taken as acceptance of the sale and agency. This implied sale establishes the woman's ownership of the slave, albeit momentarily, creating a conflict between marital ownership and right-hand ownership (since a man cannot be owned by his wife), thereby invalidating the marriage. The passage then addresses two objections to this reasoning. The first objection notes that if the master had explicitly said 'I sold and freed,' emancipation would be on his behalf, not hers, by unanimous agreement. So why should the implication yield a different result? The response distinguishes between explicit and implicit proof: implicit proof can establish something even when explicit proof does not, just as a fetus is implicitly included in the sale of its mother. The second objection argues that the ownership acquired by implication is minimal and temporary, vanishing instantly upon emancipation, and thus should not affect the marriage's validity. The response counters that when a thing is proven, it brings all its consequences, and invalidating marriage is a necessary consequence of ownership. Furthermore, the analogy with an agent purchasing his own wife is rejected because in that case ownership is directly for the principal, not for the agent, so there is no conflict. The commentary concludes that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the three imams, as found in Al-Hidayah and its commentaries.

Key Terms
الآمر (al-āmir): The one who gives the command; in this context, the free woman who requests the slave's emancipation.
المأمور (al-ma'mur): The one who receives the command; in this context, the slave's master (the mawla) who frees the slave.
الولاء (al-walā'): The bond of patronage that arises from manumission, entitling the former master to inheritance under certain conditions.
اقتضاء (iqtidā'): Implication; a method of interpreting speech to include what is necessary for its validity, even if not explicitly stated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi imams, what is the ruling on the marriage when a free woman, married to a slave, asks the slave's master to free him on her behalf for a specified price?
A) The marriage remains valid because the woman does not own the slave.
B) The marriage becomes invalid because the woman acquires ownership of her husband by implied sale.
C) The marriage becomes invalid only if the price is not given.
D) The marriage remains valid but the wala' goes to the woman.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that a marriage is invalidated when a wife acquires ownership of her husband through an implied sale, even if the ownership is temporary and by legal interpretation.

No Invalidation When No Price Specified for Emancipation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage validity when free woman requests emancipation without specifying a price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a free woman requests emancipation without a price, and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Without a price, no sale is implied; gift fails because possession is a physical act that cannot be presumed.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute. When a free woman tells the master of her slave-husband to free him on her behalf without naming a price, does the marriage become invalid? Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad say no, while Imam Abu Yusuf argues that the same logic from the previous case applies. We will dissect the reasoning behind each position and understand why the majority view is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
A free woman, married to a slave, asks the master to free him on her behalf without specifying any price. Identify the legal issue: does this imply a sale or gift that could invalidate the marriage?
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (marriage valid, wala' to master) and the ruling of Abu Yusuf (marriage invalid, same as the case with a specified price).
Note the disagreement is only on this sub-case; the previous case (with price) is agreed upon.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's reasoning
He interprets the request as an implied gift (hiba) and argues that the condition of possession (qabd) can be dropped by necessity, analogizing to the case of expiation for zihar where feeding can be delegated.
His analogy relies on the idea that the slave can receive on her behalf, similar to a pauper.
4
Analyze the majority's response
They reject the analogy: possession for a gift is a physical act required by explicit text (nass), cannot be waived or established by implication. Unlike sale (a legal construct), gift requires actual physical delivery. In the expiation case, the pauper can act as agent, but here the slave cannot because nothing is placed in his hand.
Thus, no ownership transfers to the woman; the emancipation is from the master, and wala' follows.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The mufta bihi position is that the marriage remains valid. The thamarah al-khilaf: if a price is specified, the marriage invalidates (by majority); if not, it remains valid. This distinction is crucial for fatwa.
The dispute does not affect other cases; only this specific scenario.

And if she said: 'Free him on my behalf' and did not specify a price, the marriage does not become invalid, and the wala' is for the liberator. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them). Abu Yusuf said: this and the first are the same, because he presumes transfer of ownership without compensation to validate her action, and drops the condition of possession (qabd), just as when someone owes expiation for zihar and commands another to feed on his behalf. And their evidence (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that gift (hiba) requires possession by text, so it cannot be dropped nor proven by implication, because possession is a physical act, unlike sale which is a legal transaction. And in that case (the feeding), the poor person acts as the agent of the commander in taking possession, but as for the slave, nothing falls into his hand to act as agent. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: this and the first case are the same, because he presumes transfer of ownership without compensation to validate her action, and drops the condition of possession, just as when someone owes expiation for zihar and commands another to feed on his behalf. And their evidence (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that gift requires possession by text, so it cannot be dropped nor proven by implication, because possession is a physical act, unlike sale which is a legal transaction. And in that case (the feeding), the poor person acts as the agent of the commander in taking possession, but as for the slave, nothing falls into his hand to act as agent.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents another intra-Hanafi dispute, now concerning the same scenario but without the woman specifying a price for the emancipation. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, the marriage does not become invalid and the wala' (patronage) belongs to the master who actually performs the emancipation. Imam Abu Yusuf, however, argues that this case is identical to the previous one: the woman's request should be interpreted as an implied grant (hiba) of the slave to her, followed by her freeing him. He reasons that the condition of possession (qabd) for hiba can be dispensed with by necessity, analogous to the case where a person owing expiation for zihar commands another to feed the poor on his behalf. In that case, the pauper's receipt of food is considered constructive possession on behalf of the original obligor. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad refute this by pointing out that hiba requires actual possession by explicit textual proof (Quran: 'and give [hiba]' implies transfer of possession). Possession is a physical act that cannot be waived or proven by implication (iqtida'), unlike sale which is a legal construct. In the zihar case, the poor person can act as an agent for the commander in taking possession, but in the emancipation scenario, the slave himself cannot act as an agent because nothing is being placed in his hand. Therefore, without a price, the woman's words cannot imply a sale (since no price is mentioned) and cannot imply a gift (since possession is impossible here), so no ownership transfers to her. Hence the emancipation is attributed to the master, wala' goes to him, and the marriage remains valid. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; transfer of ownership without compensation. In this context, the woman's request could be interpreted as seeking a gift of the slave, but the condition of possession prevents it.
القبض (al-qabd): Physical possession; taking control of the object of a transaction. Required for validity of gifts and certain sales.
كفارة الظهار (kaffārat al-zihār): Expiation for the pre-Islamic practice of zihar (saying to one's wife 'you are like my mother's back'). The expiation is freeing a slave or feeding the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marriage becomes invalid because of implied gift.
B) The marriage remains valid and wala' goes to the woman.
C) The marriage remains valid and wala' goes to the master.
D) The marriage becomes invalid only if the master accepts the request.

Key Takeaway
When a free woman requests emancipation of her slave-husband without a price, the Hanafi majority holds the marriage valid because no sale or gift can be implied without a clear term or the possibility of possession.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Freeing a Slave Husband Without Specifying Price: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage and wala when freeing without price.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) on the validity of marriage when a free woman instructs freeing her slave husband without a price, explain Abu Yusuf's counterargument, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Possession is a physical condition for gift that cannot be dropped, so marriage remains valid.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. When a free woman tells her slave husband's master to free him on her behalf without specifying a price, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the marriage remains valid and wala goes to the master. Abu Yusuf disagrees, equating it to an earlier case where a price was specified. The disagreement hinges on whether possession (qabd) can be dropped or implied in a gift (hibah).

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Clearly describe the case: a free woman is married to a slave; she instructs the master to free him without mentioning any price. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the marriage is not invalidated (it remains sahih) and the wala belongs to the master (the actual manumitter).
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's objection and his qiyas-based argument
Abu Yusuf argues that this case is identical to a previous one where a price was mentioned. To validate the woman's speech, an implied gift (hibah) should be assumed: the woman first receives the slave as a gift (without possession) then frees him. He compares it to zihar expiation where possession is dropped by consensus.
Abu Yusuf's reasoning uses qiyas khafi (hidden analogy).
3
Analyze the majority's response regarding possession
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that possession (qabd) is a physical act and a textual condition for gift (based on hadith 'la tasihhu al-hibatu illa maqbudah'). It cannot be dropped or established implicitly because it is a physical action, unlike the verbal pillar of sale (ijab/qabul) which can be dropped in certain transactions like bay' al-ta'ati.
Distinguish between rukn (pillar) and shart (condition): the former can sometimes be dropped, the latter cannot if it is physical.
4
Understand the distinction from the zihar expiation case
In the zihar case, the poor person receiving food acts as a deputy for the commander in taking possession, so possession is still effected. In the slave case, the slave himself cannot deputize because nothing comes into his hand to deputize. Therefore the analogy fails.
This shows how qiyas can be invalidated by a clear distinction (farq).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Yusuf's view, the marriage becomes fasid (invalid) and must be renewed; the wala goes to the woman. Under the majority view, the marriage remains valid (sahih) and the wala goes to the master. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
This difference directly affects whether the couple can continue living as husband and wife without renewing the nikah.

And if she said, 'Free him on my behalf,' and did not specify any wealth, then the marriage is not invalidated and the clientage (wala) belongs to the one who frees (the master). This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them). Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'This and the first case are the same, because he gives precedence to making possession without compensation to correct his transaction, and the consideration of possession is dropped, just as if there was upon him the expiation of zihar and he ordered someone else to feed on his behalf.' Their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) argument is that possession is a condition for gift by textual evidence, so it cannot be dropped nor established implicitly, because it is a physical action, unlike sale because it is a legal transaction. In that case (of expiation), the poor person deputizes for the commander in taking possession, but as for the slave, nothing comes into his possession to deputize for him. Explanation: The scenario is that a free woman told her slave husband's master: 'Free him on my behalf' without mentioning any price. According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the marriage is not invalidated and the clientage goes to the master. Imam Abu Yusuf says this case and the first are equal, meaning just as in the first case freedom occurred on the part of the commander and marriage was invalidated, here too freedom occurs on the part of the commander (free woman) and marriage is invalidated. Abu Yusuf's evidence: just as in the first case, to avoid the sane person's speech being useless, we considered a sale (compensated transfer) to be implied, so here to validate the commander's action, we consider a gift (uncompensated transfer) to be implied. The implied statement would be: the free woman told the master: 'First give this slave to me as a gift, then as my agent free him.' The master said: 'I did that,' meaning first I gave him to you as a gift, then as your agent I freed him. From this it becomes clear that the slave's freedom occurred on the part of the commander and the marriage became invalid. The maximum objection to Abu Yusuf's evidence is that possession is a condition for gift, and in the mentioned scenario possession by the donee (the commander) is not found, so how can this be a gift? The answer: in the first case, acceptance which is a pillar of sale was dropped, so dropping possession which is a condition for gift is all the more possible. So just as in the first case sale occurred without acceptance, here gift is complete without possession, similar to a person who owed the expiation of zihar and ordered another to feed sixty poor people on his behalf—the meaning is: first give me the food as a gift, then give it on my behalf to the poor. If the deputy does so, his expiation is paid by consensus, even though possession was not found in that gift. So just as possession condition was dropped and the gift became valid, similarly in this case, say the condition of possession is dropped and the gift of the slave occurs, then the master frees on behalf of the woman, so the marriage is invalidated. The argument of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that possession is a condition for gift due to the hadith 'No gift is valid unless taken possession,' and it is not possible to drop possession, because only that which can potentially be dropped can be dropped incidentally, but possession in gift does not admit dropping in any circumstance, unlike the pillar of sale which can be dropped (as in sale by mutual exchange where both offer and acceptance are dropped). Therefore, analogizing possession in gift to the pillar of sale is incorrect. Also, it is impossible to establish possession implicitly because possession is a physical action, and physical action is not of the same type as acceptance, so possession cannot be established within the word 'I freed.' Unlike sale because sale is a legal transaction, so it can be established within 'I freed.' In that case (of expiation), this is a response to Abu Yusuf's analogy. The gist of the response is that when the person upon whom zihar expiation is obligatory ordered another to give food to the poor on his behalf, and the deputy gave food to the poor, in that case the poor person deputizes for the commander in taking possession, then takes possession for himself, just as when zakat is given to a poor person, he takes possession as a deputy of Allah.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a free woman who is married to a slave. She instructs the slave's master to free the slave on her behalf without specifying any monetary compensation. The issue is whether this act invalidates her marriage to the slave and to whom the wala (clientage) belongs. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, the marriage remains valid and the wala belongs to the master (the actual manumitter). Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, equating this case with the earlier one where a price was specified; he holds that the marriage is invalidated and the wala goes to the woman. Both sides use legal reasoning (qiyas). Abu Yusuf argues that to validate the woman's speech, an implied gift (hibah) should be assumed, just as an implied sale was assumed in the earlier case. He analogizes to the case of zihar expiation where possession in a gift is dropped. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) counter that possession is a physical act that cannot be dropped or implied, unlike the verbal pillar of sale. They distinguish the expiation case by noting that the poor recipient acts as a deputy for the commander in taking possession, whereas a slave cannot deputize in this manner. This passage illustrates the Hanafi method of resolving conflicting analogies through istihsan and textual priority.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): Clientage; the bond of loyalty and inheritance rights between the manumitter and the freed slave.
هبة (hibah): Gift; a transfer of ownership without compensation.
قبض (qabd): Possession; physical taking of the gifted item by the donee.
إعتاق (i'taq): Freeing a slave; manumission.
كفارة ظهار (kaffarat al-zihar): Expiation for zihar (unlawful comparison of wife to mother); consists of freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a free woman tells her slave husband's master 'Free him on my behalf' without mentioning any price, what is the ruling?
A) The marriage is invalidated and wala goes to the woman.
B) The marriage remains valid and wala goes to the master.
C) The marriage is invalidated and wala goes to the master.
D) The marriage remains valid and wala goes to the woman.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, freeing a slave without specifying a price does not invalidate the marriage, because possession in gift cannot be dropped or implied.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Free Woman's Command to Free Slave Husband
Intra-Hanafi dispute on implied transfer in slave emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of marriage when a free woman commands her slave husband's master to free him without payment, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, including the role of possession (qabd) in hiba.

Core Idea
A free woman's command to free her slave husband does not invalidate the marriage unless an implied gift or sale is established with possession.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the legal effect of a free woman's command to her slave husband's master. The core question is whether such a command implies a transfer of ownership (hiba) that would invalidate the marriage. Understanding this dispute requires careful analysis of the conditions of hiba and the limits of qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the case and the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Identify the scenario: a free woman tells her slave husband's master to free him without mentioning payment. The ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that the marriage remains valid and wala goes to the master.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's opposing position and his proof
Abu Yusuf argues that the marriage is invalidated because an implied hiba (gift) from the master to the wife must be assumed to give effect to her command. He uses qiyas to the earlier case where a sale was implied when payment was mentioned.
3
Analyze the objection to Abu Yusuf's qiyas: the condition of possession (qabd)
The objection is that hiba requires possession (qabd), which is a physical act that cannot be implied or dropped. Unlike acceptance in sale, possession cannot be dispensed with. The analogy to kaffara is also refuted.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) because the condition of possession is essential and cannot be waived, making Abu Yusuf's qiyas invalid.

پہلے اپنے لئے قبضہ کرتا ہے پس اس صورت میں ہبہ بغیر قبضہ کے نہیں رہا اور ر ہا غلام تو مامور نے جب اس کو آزاد کیا تو اس کے قبضہ میں کوئی چیز واقع نہیں ہوتی تا کہ وہ غلام آمر کی طرف سے نائب بن سکے۔ پس اس صورت میں ہبہ بلا قبضہ کے رہا۔ پس جب فقیر قبضہ میں نائب ہے اور غلام نائب نہیں ہے ۔ تو ایک کو دوسرے پر قیاس کرنا بھی درست نہیں ہے۔
Book of Nikah: So that he becomes a deputy on behalf of the commander (free woman). Sharh: The explanation of the situation is that a free woman told her slave husband's master to free him on her behalf without mentioning money. According to the two sides (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the marriage does not become invalid, and the wala' (clientage) goes to the master. Imam Abu Yusuf says that this case and the first are the same: just as in the first case emancipation occurred on behalf of the commander and the marriage became invalid, so here emancipation occurs on behalf of the commander (free woman) and the marriage becomes invalid. Abu Yusuf's proof: just as in the first case, to save a rational person's speech from being futile, an implied transfer for compensation (sale) was assumed, so here, to validate the act of a rational commander, an implied transfer without compensation (hiba) is assumed. The implied formulation would be that the free woman told the master: first gift this slave to me, then as my agent free him. The master said: I did so—first I gifted the slave to you, then as your agent I freed him. Thus emancipation is attributed to the commander and the marriage becomes invalid. The objection to Abu Yusuf's proof is that possession (qabd) is a condition for hiba, and here no possession was taken. The response: In the first case, acceptance (qabul) in sale was dropped, so possession, being a condition for hiba, can be dropped a fortiori. Just as a sale without acceptance is valid, so hiba without possession is valid. Analogous to a person obliged to perform kaffara for zihar who commands another to feed sixty poor on his behalf—the meaning is: first gift me the food, then feed the poor on my behalf. If the agent does so, the kaffara is valid, even without possession in the hiba. So here, the condition of possession is dropped, the gift of the slave occurs, then the master frees on behalf of the woman, and the marriage becomes invalid. The two sides' proof: possession is a condition for hiba due to the hadith 'No gift is valid unless taken possession of', and possession cannot be dropped because only that which is susceptible to dropping can be dropped, and possession is not susceptible. Unlike the rukn of sale which can be dropped, as in mu'atah sale where offer and acceptance are both dropped. So analogy of possession in hiba to the rukn of sale is invalid. Also, possession cannot be implied because it is a physical act, not of the same category as acceptance, so cannot be established within the scope of 'I freed'. Unlike sale which is a legal transaction and can be established within 'I freed'. The response to Abu Yusuf's analogy: in the kaffara case, the poor person takes possession as a deputy of the commander, then for himself. So hiba was not without possession. But for the slave, when the agent frees him, no possession occurs for him to act as deputy. So the analogy fails.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the emancipation of a slave husband by a free wife through a command to the master. The two sides (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the marriage remains valid because no explicit gift is mentioned, so the emancipation is directly from the master, preserving the marriage. Imam Abu Yusuf argues that to give legal effect to the wife's command, an implied gift (hiba) from the master to the wife must be assumed, followed by her emancipation, which would dissolve the marriage. The detailed reasoning involves qiyas (analogy) to the first case discussed earlier, where a similar command implied a sale. The objection centers on the condition of possession (qabd) for hiba. Abu Yusuf responds that just as acceptance can be dispensed with in sale, possession can be dispensed with in hiba by necessity. The other side counters that possession is a physical act, not a legal element that can be dropped, unlike acceptance. A counter-analogy is drawn to kaffara for zihar, but the text refutes it by noting that in kaffara the recipient takes possession as a deputy, whereas in the slave's case no such possession occurs. Thus the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is upheld: the marriage is not invalidated.

Key Terms
آمر (aamir): The free woman who commanded the master to free the slave.
ہبہ (hiba): A gift without compensation; here, an assumed gift of the slave from master to wife.
قبضہ (qabd): Physical possession; required for hiba to be valid.
ولاء معتق (wala' al-mu'tiq): Clientage established by emancipation; the right of inheritance and responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The woman is the emancipator; the marriage is invalidated.
B) The master is the emancipator; the marriage remains valid.

Key Takeaway
When a free woman commands her slave husband's master to free him without payment, the majority Hanafi view holds that the marriage remains valid because possession (qabd) is essential for a hiba and cannot be implied.

Nikah — Marriage

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Non-Muslim Marriages After Conversion
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of pre-conversion marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the validity of a pre-conversion marriage lacking witnesses or during iddah, and apply the criterion of ijma vs ikhtilaf to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Pre-conversion marriages are upheld unless they violate a universally agreed prohibition.

When non-Muslims convert to Islam, questions arise about the validity of their prior marriages, especially if those marriages did not meet Islamic conditions like witnesses or observing iddah. The Hanafi school has a nuanced approach: it distinguishes between prohibitions that are agreed upon by all Muslims (ijma) and those that are disputed. This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi debate, focusing on the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Zufar, and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three main Hanafi views: Abu Hanifa (upheld in both cases), Zufar (invalid in both), Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (upheld for no witnesses, invalid during iddah).
Note that the Sahibayn split the cases based on the nature of the prohibition.
2
Analyze the basis of each position
Examine the key argument: Zufar relies on the generality of Shariah (khitabat 'ammah); the Sahibayn use the ijma/ikhtilaf distinction; Abu Hanifa focuses on lack of address (khitab) and absence of belief.
Abu Hanifa argues non-Muslims are not addressed by Shariah's detailed rights (huquq al-shar').
3
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: if the marriage is valid (Abu Hanifa), the couple remains married; if invalid (Zufar), they must separate; the Sahibayn's view leads to differential treatment.
This shows how abstract usul principles affect real-life rulings.
4
Identify the mufta bihi and tarjih
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view first and supports it, indicating it is the relied-upon position. Understand why it prevails over the other views.
The later explanation emphasizes that non-Muslims are not bound by rulings they do not believe in.

Source Text

باب نكاح اهل الشرك
ترجمه مشرکین کے نکاح کے بیان میں
کسی کافر نے بغیر گواہوں کے یا کسی کافر کی عدت میں نکاح کیا اور یہ ان کے دین میں جائز ہے پھر دونوں مسلمان ہو گئے پہلا نکاح برقرار ہے یا نہیں، اقوال فقہاء
و اذا تزوج الكافر بغير شهود او فى عدة كافر و ذالك فى دينهم جائزثم اسلما أقرأ عليه وهذا عند ابي حنيفةً و قال زفر النكاح فاسد في الوجهين الا انه لا يتعرض لهم قبل الاسلام والمرافعة الى الحكام وقال ابويوسف و محمد رحمهما الله فى الوجه الاول كما قال ابو حنيفة وفى الوجه الثانى كما قال زفر رحمه الله له ان الخطابات عامة على مامر من قبل فتلزمهم وانما لا يتعرض لهم لذمتهم اعراضا لاتقرير او اذا ترافعوا او اسلموا والحرمة قائمة وجب التفريق ولهما ان حرمة نكاح المعتدة مجمع عليها فكانوا ملتزمين لها وحرمة النكاح بغير شهود مختلف فيه ولم يلتزموا احكامنا بجميع الاختلافات ولابي حنيفة ان الحرمة لا يمكن اثباتها حقا للشرع لانهم لا يخاطبون بحقوقه ولاوجه الى ايجاب العدة حقا للزوج لانه لا يعتقده بخلاف ما اذا كانت تحت مسلم لانه يعتقده واذاصح النكاح فحالة المرافعة والاسلام حالة البقاء و الشهادة ليست شرط فيها وكذا العدة لا تنافيها كالمنكوحة اذاو طئت بشبهة
ترجمہ اور اگر کافر نے بغیر گواہوں کے نکاح کیا یا کافر کی عدت میں تھی حالانکہ یہ ان کے دین میں جائز ہے پھر دونوں مرد و عورت مسلمان ہو گئے۔ تو اسی نکاح پر باقی رکھے جائیں گے اور یہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے۔ اور امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ نکاح دونوں صورتوں میں فاسد ہے مگر یہ کہ ان سے تعرض نہ کرے۔ اسلام اور مرافعہ الی الحکام سے پہلے اور امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد نے فرمایا پہلی صورت میں جیسا کہ ابو حنیفہ نے کہا اور دوسری صورت میں جیسا کہ امام زفر نے کہا۔ امام زفر کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ خطابات شرع عام ہیں۔ چنانچہ پہلے گذر چکا تو وہ کافروں کو بھی لازم ہوں گے اور ان کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے ان سے تعرض نہ کرنا اعراض کے طور پر ہے کہ برقرار رکھنے کے طور پر اور جب انہوں نے (حکام کے پاس) مرافعہ کیا یا مسلمان ہو گئے اور حرمت موجود ہے تو تفریق واجب ہو گئی اور صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ معتدہ کے نکاح کا حرام ہونا متفق علیہ ہے۔ پس کفار ا سکے ماننے کا التزام کرنے والے ہوں گے۔ اور بغیر گواہوں کے نکاح کی حرمت مختلف فیہ ہے اور انہوں نے تمام اختلافات کے ساتھ ہمارے احکام کا التزام نہیں کیا ہے۔ اور امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ حرمت کو ثابت کرنا بطور حق شرع کے ممکن نہیں ۔ اسلئے کہ وہ حقوق شرع کے مخاطب نہیں ہیں اور کوئی وجہ نہیں عدت واجب کرنا بطور حق زوجہ کے ۔ اسلئے کہ زوج اس کا اعتقاد نہیں رکھتا ہے۔ بخلاف اس کے جب ( کتابیہ عورت ) کسی مسلمان کے تحت ہو کیونکہ مسلمان اس کا اعتقاد رکھتا ہے
Chapter on the marriage of polytheists. Explanation regarding the marriage of polytheists: If a disbeliever marries without witnesses or during the waiting period of a disbeliever, and that is permissible in their religion, then both become Muslims, is the first marriage upheld or not? Statements of jurists: 'And if a disbeliever marries without witnesses or during the waiting period of a disbeliever, and that is permissible in their religion, then both become Muslims, it is upheld (upon him). This is according to Abu Hanifa. And Zufar said: The marriage is invalid in both cases, except that they are not interfered with before Islam and before litigation before judges. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: In the first case, as Abu Hanifa said; in the second case, as Zufar said. Zufar's proof: The addresses (of Shariah) are general, as previously established, so they bind them (the disbelievers). They are not interfered with due to their covenant (dhimma) as a matter of turning away, not as confirmation. When they litigate (before judges) or become Muslims and the prohibition exists, separation becomes obligatory. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): The prohibition of marrying a woman in iddah is agreed upon (ijma), so they (disbelievers) are held to it. But the prohibition of marriage without witnesses is disputed (among Muslims), and they do not adopt our rulings with all their differences. For Abu Hanifa: The prohibition cannot be established as a right of the Shariah because they are not addressed by its rights, and there is no way to make the iddah obligatory as a right of the husband because he does not believe in it. Unlike when a (scriptuary woman) is under a Muslim, because he believes in it. When the marriage is valid, then during litigation and after Islam it remains in effect. Testimony is not a condition for it (in their case), nor does the iddah contradict it, like a married woman who has intercourse under a mistaken claim (shubha).
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage discusses a key Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a marriage contracted by non-Muslims before conversion. There are three positions: (1) Abu Hanifa: the marriage is upheld in both cases (no witnesses or during iddah) because non-Muslims are not addressed by the specific rulings of Shariah (e.g., iddah) unless they believe in them. The prohibition of marriage without witnesses is disputed (ikhtilaf), so they are not held to it. (2) Zufar: the marriage is invalid in both cases because the Shariah addresses all, and prohibition exists; but they are not interfered with until they convert or bring the case to Islamic court. (3) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: distinction between the two cases. The marriage without witnesses is upheld (as per Abu Hanifa) because its prohibition is not agreed upon (mukhtalaf fih), while marriage during iddah is invalid (as per Zufar) because its prohibition is agreed upon (mujma' alayh) and thus binding on all. The passage provides reasoning for each: Zufar holds Shariah is universal; the Sahibayn use the criterion of ijma vs. ikhtilaf; Abu Hanifa focuses on the lack of address (khitab) and belief. The commentary upholds Abu Hanifa's view? Actually the text presents all, but the phrase 'و هذا عند ابي حنيفة' may indicate the preferred view. The later clause 'و اذا صح النكاح' supports Abu Hanifa's position that the marriage is valid.

Key Terms
عدة كافر (iddat kafir): The waiting period observed by a non-Muslim woman after divorce or death of her husband, according to her own religion.
مختلف فيه (mukhtalaf fih): A ruling over which there is disagreement among Muslim jurists.
مجمع عليه (mujma' alayh): A ruling upon which there is consensus among Muslim scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, what is the ruling if a non-Muslim marries during the iddah of a non-Muslim (permissible in their religion), then both convert to Islam?
A) The marriage is upheld because the prohibition is disputed.
B) The marriage is invalid because the prohibition is agreed upon among Muslims.
C) The marriage is upheld only if they had witnesses.
D) The marriage is valid but must be renewed after conversion.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, pre-conversion marriages are upheld unless they violate a prohibition that is agreed upon by all Muslims (ijma); disputed prohibitions (ikhtilaf) are not retroactively enforced.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
12 min
Ruling on Marriage of Polytheists Upon Embracing Islam
Intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage validity after conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Zufar, and the Sahibayn regarding the validity of marriages contracted by disbelievers in ways prohibited in Islam but permissible in their religion, and explain the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
A marriage contracted by disbelievers in a manner they consider valid is confirmed upon Islam according to Abu Hanifa, but Zufar and Sahibayn differ based on the nature of the prohibition.

When a disbeliever marries without witnesses or during 'iddah, and that is permissible in their religion, what happens when both embrace Islam? The Hanafi school presents a nuanced dispute among its own imams. This lesson walks through the three positions and their evidence, showing how each imam uses different usul principles to arrive at his ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the three positions
Read the case: a disbeliever marries without witnesses or during 'iddah, then both become Muslim. Note the three answers: Abu Hanifa says valid; Zufar says invalid in both; Sahibayn differentiate between the two scenarios.
The Sahibayn agree with Abu Hanifa on the witnesses case but with Zufar on the 'iddah case.
2
Examine Zufar's evidence
Zufar argues that the general addresses of the Shari'ah (Quran 2:228 and Hadith 'La nikaha illa bi shuhood') bind disbelievers as well. The dhimmah only delays interference, but upon conversion or litigation, the prohibition is enforced.
He relies on the generality of the commands.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's evidence
They distinguish: the prohibition of marriage during 'iddah is agreed upon (mujma' alayh), so disbelievers are committed; the prohibition of marriage without witnesses is disputed, so they are not committed to it.
They use the principle of ijma' to differentiate.
4
Examine Abu Hanifa's evidence
Abu Hanifa argues that disbelievers are not addressed by the obligations of Shari'ah as religious duties. The husband does not believe in 'iddah, so it cannot be enforced as his right. Therefore, the marriage remains valid upon conversion.
His key premise: they are not mukhatabun (addressees) of the detailed rulings.
5
Determine the practical consequence and the relied-upon position
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, no renewal needed; under Zufar, the marriage must be renewed in both cases; under Sahibayn, only the 'iddah case requires renewal. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's.
This is stated as 'aquirrara 'alayhi' (it is confirmed).
