صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اصل نکاح تو باطل تھا لیکن عقد ذمہ کی وجہ سے تعرض نہیں کیا جا سکتا تھا۔ پس جب ان دونوں میں سے ایک نے مرافعہ یعنی حکم اسلام طلب کیا اور حکم اسلام کیلئے مطیع ہو گیا تو ان دونوں میں تفریق کر دی جائے گی جیسا کہ اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک مسلمان ہو جاتا تو بالا تفاق تفریق کر دی جاتی۔ پس ان دونوں میں سے ایک کا مسلمان ہونا ایسا ہے جیسا کہ دونوں کا مسلمان ہونا۔ اسی طرح ان دونوں میں سے ایک کا مرافعہ کرنا ایسا ہے جیسا کہ دونوں کا مرافعہ کرنا۔ اور ان دونوں کے مرافعہ کرنے کی صورت میں بالاتفاق تفریق کی جاتی۔ لہذا ایک کے مرافعہ کرنے کی صورت میں بھی تفریق کر دی جائیگی۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل اور دونوں صورتوں میں وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ اصل نکاح تو صحیح تھا اور ان دونوں میں سے ایک کا قاضی کے پاس مرافعہ کرنا اور حکم اسلام کو طلب کرنا دوسرے ساتھی کے خلاف اسکے حق کو باطل کرنے میں حجت نہیں ہوگا بلکہ اسکا اعتقاد دوسرے کے اعتقاد کے معارض ہے اور مرج نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے ایک کو دوسرے پر ترجیح بھی نہیں ہوگی ۔ لہذا حکم صحت سابقہ حالت پر باقی رہے گا۔ اسکے برخلاف جب ان دونوں میں سے ایک نے اسلام قبول کر لیا تو تفریق کر دیجائے گی کیونکہ اسلام غالب رہتا ہے مغلوب نہیں۔ لہذا مصر علی الکفر کا اعتقاد مسلمان کے اسلام کے معارض نہیں ہوسکتا ہے۔ بلکہ مسلمان کے اسلام کو ترجیح دے کر دونوں میں تفریق کر دی جائے گی۔ اور اگر دونوں نے قاضی کے پاس مرافعہ کیا اور حکم اسلام طلب کیا تو بالا تفاق تفریق کر دی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ ان دونوں کا مرافعہ کرنا ایسا ہے جیسا کہ دونوں کا کسی تیسرے کو حکم بنانا اور یہ دونوں اگر کسی کو حکم بناتے اور اس سے حکم اسلام طلب کرتے تو حکم کیلئے جائز تھا کہ ان دونوں کے درمیان تفریق کر دے۔ پس قاضی عموم ولایت کی وجہ سے زیادہ مستحق ہے کہ ان دونوں میں تفریق کر دے۔ اسی کی طرف اللہ تعالیٰ نے اشارہ فرمایا ہے فسان جاؤك فاحكم بينهم بما انزل الله الآیة
Translation: Then (if one of a Magian man and woman becomes Muslim, separation will be made between both, and if one of them makes a reference (murafa'ah) separation will not be made, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, there is difference with Sahibayn. The difference is that the entitlement of one of them is not invalidated by the other's reference, because his belief does not change by that. Nevertheless, the belief of the one persisting in disbelief is not opposed to the Islam of the Muslim, because Islam prevails, it is not overcome. And if both made reference, then by consensus separation will be made, because both making reference is like both appointing a judge. Explanation of the case: A Magian married one of his close relatives, then one of them accepted Islam, then by agreement separation is made. If one of them approached the judge (sought Islamic ruling), according to Imam Sahib separation is not made, but according to Sahibayn separation is made. Sahibayn's proof: The original marriage was invalid, but due to the covenant of protection, it could not be interfered with. So when one of them made reference, i.e., sought Islamic judgment, and became obedient for Islamic judgment, then separation will be made between them, just as if one of them became Muslim, then by agreement separation would be made. So one of them becoming Muslim is like both becoming Muslim. Similarly, one of them making reference is like both making reference. And in the case of both making reference, separation is made by consensus. Therefore, in the case of one making reference, separation should also be made. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof and the reason for difference in both cases: The original marriage was valid, and one of them going to the judge and seeking Islamic ruling is not a proof to invalidate the right of the other partner, rather his belief is opposed to the other's belief, and due to lack of preference (tarjih), one cannot be preferred over the other. Therefore, the ruling of validity remains on the previous state. In contrast, when one of them accepts Islam, separation is made because Islam prevails, it is not overcome. So the belief of the one persisting in disbelief cannot oppose the Islam of the Muslim; rather, preference is given to the Muslim's Islam and separation is made. And if both approached the judge and sought Islamic ruling, then by consensus separation is made, because both making reference is like both making a third party as judge, and if both make someone a judge and seek Islamic ruling from him, it is permissible for the judge to make separation between them. So the judge, due to general authority, is more entitled to make separation between them. Allah Almighty has indicated this: 'So if they come to you, judge between them by what Allah has revealed.'
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the separation of a Magian couple when one spouse converts to Islam or when one spouse seeks a ruling from the judge (murafa'ah). The author first states the ruling: if one converts, separation is made unanimously; if one seeks a ruling, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, separation is not made, but according to the Two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), separation is made. The passage then details the reasoning of both sides. Sahibayn argue by analogy: one's reference to judge is like one's conversion, and since conversion triggers separation, so should reference. They also argue that the original marriage was invalid but tolerated; the reference shows acceptance of Islamic law, so separation is required. Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes: the original marriage is valid (as per his view that Magian marriages based on a semblance of validity are valid ab initio), and one partner's reference does not affect the other's belief; there is no preponderance (tarjih) to favor one over the other, so the status quo remains. However, when one converts, Islam's dominance overrides the other's disbelief, so separation occurs. When both refer, it is like both submitting to a third-party judgment, so separation is made. The passage also cites the Quranic verse instructing judgment. The practical difference is whether the judge must separate them upon one spouse's request alone; Abu Hanifa's view gives more weight to the original validity of the marriage.

Key Terms
مرافعہ (murafa'ah): Seeking a ruling from the judge; referring a dispute to the court.
مجوسی (majusi): A Magian, follower of Zoroastrianism.
عقد ذمہ ('aqd al-dhimmah): The covenant of protection granted to non-Muslims living under Islamic rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The original marriage was invalid.
B) The other spouse's belief does not change, and there is no preponderance.
C) Islam always prevails.
D) Both parties must agree to dissolve.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between conversion (which dissolves) and mere reference to the judge (which does not), due to the lack of preponderance in the latter.

The Ruling on an Apostate's Marriage: Prohibition, Proof, and Rationale
States that an apostate cannot marry any woman, with proof and objections answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an apostate's marriage is invalid, explain the primary dalil (hadith), and distinguish the ruling from cases of murderers and adulterers.

Core Idea
An apostate's marriage is invalid because he is wajib al-qatl and must reflect; marriage distracts from repentance.

This passage from al-Hidayah examines the prohibition of marriage for an apostate. It not only states the ruling but also engages with potential objections, demonstrating how Hanafi jurists defend their position using both textual evidence and rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Act
The passage states that an apostate (murtad) cannot marry any woman—Muslim, non-Muslim, or fellow apostate. Identify the precise ruling: the marriage contract is invalid (batil) and prohibited (haram).
The same applies to an apostate woman: she cannot be married by anyone.
2
Locate the Primary Dalil
The author cites the hadith 'Man baddala dinahu faqtuluhu' (Whoever changes his religion, kill him). This shows the apostate is wajib al-qatl, which is the basis for the ruling.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its dalalah (meaning) is clear and qat'i in indicating the death penalty.
3
Understand the Rationale
The apostate is given a three-day respite (tamul) to reflect and repent. Marriage would distract him from this essential reflection, so it is not legislated.
The same rationale applies to the apostate woman: she is detained for reflection, and marital duties would hinder that.
4
Analyze the Objections and Hanafi Responses
Two objections are raised: (1) A murderer (qatil amd) is also wajib al-qatl but can marry; (2) A married adulterer (zani muhsin) is wajib al-qatl (stoning) but his marriage is valid. The Hanafi response distinguishes each case: pardon is possible for the murderer, and witnesses may retract for the adulterer, so their executions are not certain. The apostate's execution is certain unless he repents, and he must not be distracted.
These responses show that the ruling is not based solely on the death penalty but on the combination of death penalty and the need for undistracted reflection.
5
Conclude with the Underlying Principle
Marriage is legislated for its interests (masalih): dwelling, companionship, procreation. With an apostate, these interests cannot be realized because he is not a stable member of Islamic society. Hence, marriage is invalid.
This teleological reasoning reinforces the ruling.

یعنی جس شخص نے اپنا دین بدل ڈالا اس کو قتل کر دو۔ والامهال ضرورة التامل سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ مرتد چونکہ واجب القتل ہے لہذا اس کو مہلت نہ دینی چاہئے۔ جواب مرتد کو تین دن کی مہلت اسلئے دی گئی تا کہ وہ غور و فکر کر کے ان شبہات کو دور کرے جو اس کو اسلام کے بارے میں پیش آئے ہیں۔ اور نکاح چونکہ اس کو غور و فکر سے غافل کر دیتا ہے۔ اسلئے نکاح مرتد کے حق میں مشروع نہیں کیا گیا۔ اس دلیل پر ہونے کے بعد مرتد ہوا ہے۔ پس اس کے باوجود اس کا ارتدادا اس کے گمان میں کسی قومی شبہ کی وجہ سے ہے۔ مگر اشکال اب بھی باقی ہے۔ کیونکہ زانی محصن جس کا زنا بعینہ یا اقرار سے ثابت ہوا ہے واجب القتل ہے۔ یعنی سنگسار کر کے ہلاک کر دینا ضروری ہے۔ اسکے باوجود اس کا نکاح درست ہے۔ حالانکہ آپ کے بیان کے مطابق درست نہ ہونا چاہئے ۔ جواب ممکن ہے کہ گواہ گواہی سے رجوع کرلیں اور رجم ٹل جائے۔
دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ مرتدہ کے ساتھ نہ مسلمان نکاح کرے اور نہ کا فر۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مرتدہ کو محبوس کیا جاتا ہے تا کہ وہ غور وفکر کر کے اپنے شبہات دور کرلے۔ اور شوہر کی خدمت اس کو غور و فکر کرنے سے غافل کر دے گی۔ اسلئے اسکے حق میں بھی نکاح مشروع نہیں کیا گیا ہے۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مصالح نکاح ان دونوں کے درمیان حاصل نہیں ہو سکتے ہیں اور نکاح لذاتہ مشروع نہیں کیا گیا۔ بلکہ نکاح کے مصالح کیلئے مشروع کیا گیا ہے۔ نکاح کے مصالح مثلا سکنی، ازدواج، توالد و تناسل وغیرہ۔
Translation: And it is not permissible for an apostate to marry a Muslim woman, nor a disbelieving woman, nor an apostate woman, because he is deserving of execution, and the respite is necessary for reflection, and marriage would occupy him from that, so it is not legislated for him. Similarly, an apostate woman should not be married by a Muslim nor a disbeliever, because she is held for reflection, and the service of the husband would occupy her from that, and because the interests of marriage cannot be realized between them, and marriage was not legislated for its own sake but for its interests. Commentary: The case is that an apostate is not permitted to marry a Muslim woman, a disbelieving woman, or an apostate woman. The proof is that the apostate is deserving of execution due to apostasy itself, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' The phrase 'and the respite is necessary for reflection' is an answer to an objection: since the apostate deserves death, he should not be given respite. The answer is that he is given three days to reflect and remove the doubts that occurred to him about Islam. Since marriage would distract him from reflection, it is not legislated for him. An objection arises: the intentional murderer (qatil amd) is also deserving of execution by retaliation (qisas), so his marriage should also be invalid, but it is permissible. The answer is that in qisas, pardon is recommended; Allah says: 'But if anyone is granted remission by the brother of the slain, then let the pursuit be with fairness.' Thus, when pardon is recommended, the murderer is not necessarily executed. In contrast, the apostate will generally return from his apostasy because he apostatized after being aware of the beauties of Islam; his apostasy is due to some doubt. However, an objection remains: the married adulterer (zani muhsin) whose adultery is proven by sighting or confession is also deserving of execution (stoning), yet his marriage is valid. The answer is that it is possible that the witnesses may retract their testimony, and the stoning is averted. The second case: an apostate woman should not be married by a Muslim nor a disbeliever. The proof is that she is detained for reflection, and the service of the husband would distract her from that. Therefore, marriage is not legislated for her. The second proof is that the interests of marriage cannot be realized between them, and marriage was legislated not for its own sake but for its interests, such as dwelling, companionship, procreation, etc.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on the marriage of an apostate (murtad). It states that an apostate cannot marry any woman—Muslim, non-Muslim, or fellow apostate—because he is wajib al-qatl (deserving of death). He is given a three-day respite for reflection (tamul), and marriage would distract him from this, so it is not legislated for him. Similarly, an apostate woman cannot be married by either a Muslim or a non-Muslim, for the same reason: she is detained for reflection, and marital duties would hinder that. The passage then addresses two objections: (1) Why is a murderer’s marriage valid when he is also wajib al-qatl? Because qisas can be waived by pardon, so he is not necessarily executed. (2) Why is a married adulterer’s marriage valid despite being wajib al-qatl (stoning)? Because witnesses might retract, averting the punishment. The underlying rationale is that the primary purpose of marriage (sukun, companionship, procreation) cannot be achieved with an apostate, as they are not stable in Islamic society. Thus, marriage is not legislated for them.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtad): A person who has renounced Islam after being Muslim.
واجب القتل (wajib al-qatl): Deserving of execution by law.
تامل (ta'ammul): Reflection, contemplation, specifically for an apostate to consider returning to Islam.
زانی محصن (zani muhsin): A married adulterer (or one who has been married) liable for stoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the apostate's disbelief invalidates the marriage contract.
D) Because the apostate cannot financially support a wife.

Key Takeaway
An apostate's marriage is invalid because the primary purpose of marriage cannot be realized, and it distracts from the obligatory reflection period.

Child Follows the Better Religion of Parents
Ruling on child's religion when parents differ in faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the religion of a child when parents are of different faiths and explain the principle of nazar (consideration) that supports it.

Core Idea
A child follows the better religion of the parents due to consideration (nazar) for the child's welfare.

This passage addresses a critical issue in family law: what religion does a child follow when parents belong to different faiths? The Hanafi school prioritises the child's best interest, applying the principle of nazar (consideration) to determine the child's religious status. This ruling has significant implications for custody, marriage, and other legal matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage covers three scenarios: (1) one parent Muslim, other non-Muslim; (2) one parent converts to Islam while child is young; (3) both non-Muslim – one Kitabi, one Magian.
Each scenario has a specific ruling.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For (1) and (2), the child is Muslim. For (3), the child is Kitabi (Jewish/Christian).
The ruling is consistent: the child gets the better religion.
3
Identify the underlying principle
The principle is nazar (consideration for the child's welfare). It is wajib to give the child the religion that is better for them.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) rather than a textual proof.
4
Recognize the Shafi'i disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees on scenario (3) because he sees the two non-Muslim religions as equally invalid (ta'arud).
The Hanafi response is to apply tarjih (preponderance) based on nazar.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
A Hanafi judge rules the child as Kitabi, affecting marriage and inheritance; a Shafi'i judge would leave the child's religion unresolved until puberty.
This shows the real-world impact of the ikhtilaf.

If one of the spouses is Muslim, then the child is on his religion. Similarly, if one of them becomes Muslim and he has a young child, his child becomes Muslim by his Islam, because in making him a follower of him there is consideration for him. And if one of them is a Scriptuary (Ahl al-Kitab) and the other a Magian, then the child is a Scriptuary, because there is a kind of consideration for him since Magianism is worse than it. And al-Shafi'i opposes us in this due to conflict (of proofs), and we have established the preponderance (tarjih).
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on the religion of a child when parents are of different faiths. If one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim. If a parent becomes Muslim after having a young child, that child also becomes Muslim automatically, because it is in the child's best interest to follow the better religion. If both parents are non-Muslim, one being a Kitabi (Jew/Christian) and the other a Magian, the child follows the Kitabi religion because it is less worse (sharr minhu). The passage notes that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees on the latter point due to conflicting considerations, but the Hanafi school has established a preference (tarjih) based on what is better for the child. The underlying principle is the child's welfare (nazar lahu).

Key Terms
کتابی (kitabi): A follower of a revealed scripture, i.e., Jewish or Christian.
مجوسی (majusi): A Magian, Zoroastrian.
نظر (nazar): Consideration, looking after the child's welfare.

Key Takeaway

Prohibition of Marriage for an Apostate
Ruling with proof on marriage prohibition for apostates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage of an apostate and explain the proof from hadith and the istihsan-based response to objections.

Core Idea
An apostate's marriage is invalid because he is wajib al-qatl and marriage distracts from the required reflection for repentance.

Marriage is a sacred contract with many benefits, but when one of the parties becomes an apostate, the Hanafi school rules that the marriage cannot proceed. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof from the hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him,' and the school's response to objections based on analogy with other wajib al-qatl cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Scope
Determine whom the apostate cannot marry (Muslim woman, disbelieving woman, apostate woman) and whether this applies to both male and female apostates.
The prohibition is absolute; no type of marriage is permitted.
2
Examine the Primary Proof
Analyze the hadith 'Man baddala dinahu faqtuluhu' and how it establishes that the apostate is wajib al-qatl. Then see why this status prevents marriage (distraction from reflection).
The three-day respite is for reflection, and marriage would preoccupy the apostate.
3
Analyze Objections and Hanafi Responses
Consider two objections: (1) the deliberate murderer is also wajib al-qatl yet can marry; (2) the muhsan adulterer is also wajib al-qatl yet can marry. Follow the Hanafi responses distinguishing pardon and retraction possibilities.
The key distinction is the permanence of the death sentence – apostate is not subject to pardon.
4
Understand the Underlying Rationale
Recognize that marriage was legislated for its benefits (masalih) such as tranquility and procreation. These benefits cannot be achieved in a marriage involving an apostate, so the marriage is not prescribed.
This is an istihsan based on the maqasid of marriage.

The Arabic matn: 'It distracts her and because the benefits cannot be achieved between them, and marriage was not legislated for itself but for its benefits.' Commentary: It is not permissible for an apostate to marry a Muslim woman, nor a disbelieving woman, nor an apostate woman, because the apostate is wajib al-qatl (liable to execution). The respite for reflection is given, and marriage would distract him from reflection. Thus marriage is not prescribed for him. Similarly, a Muslim man should not marry an apostate woman, nor a disbeliever, because the apostate woman is imprisoned for reflection and serving her husband would distract her. Also, the benefits of marriage cannot be achieved between them, and marriage was not legislated for itself but for its benefits. Explanation: The apostate is not permitted to marry a Muslim woman, a disbelieving woman, or an apostate woman. The proof: the apostate is wajib al-qatl due to apostasy, because the Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' The respite (of three days) is given for deliberation, and marriage distracts from that, so it is not prescribed. Objection: A deliberate murderer (qatil amd) is also wajib al-qatl by retaliation, yet his marriage is valid. Answer: In retaliation, pardon (afw) is recommended, so he is not permanently wajib al-qatl; whereas the apostate rarely returns from apostasy. Second scenario: A Muslim man should not marry an apostate woman, nor a disbeliever, because she is imprisoned for reflection, and serving her husband would distract her. Also, the benefits of marriage cannot be achieved, and marriage was legislated for its benefits, such as tranquility, companionship, procreation, etc.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and discussion from the Hanafi commentary on al-Hidayah. It addresses the prohibition of marriage for an apostate (murtadd). The author begins by stating the ruling: an apostate cannot marry a Muslim woman, a disbelieving woman, or another apostate woman. The reason given is that the apostate is wajib al-qatl (liable to execution) because of the hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' However, he is given a three-day respite for reflection to remove doubts. Marriage would distract from this reflection, so it is not prescribed for him. The author then deals with two objections: first, that a deliberate murderer (qatil amd) is also wajib al-qatl yet can marry; the answer is that pardon in retaliation is recommended, so the murderer is not permanently wajib al-qatl, whereas the apostate rarely returns. Second, that a muhsan adulterer (zani muhsan) is also wajib al-qatl yet can marry; the answer is that witnesses may retract, so stoning may be avoided. Then the author discusses the case of the apostate woman: she is imprisoned for reflection, and serving a husband would distract her, so she too cannot marry. The overarching reason is that the benefits of marriage (sukun, companionship, procreation) cannot be achieved between an apostate and a Muslim or between two apostates, and marriage was legislated for these benefits, not as an end in itself. This is a pure Hanafi position based on istihsan (juristic preference) derived from the general purpose of marriage and the special situation of apostates.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who has left Islam, whether by words or actions; a male apostate.
واجب القتل (wajib al-qatl): One who is obligated to be executed under Islamic law, here referring to the apostate after the period of reflection.
مصالح نکاح (masalih al-nikah): The beneficial purposes of marriage, such as tranquility, companionship, and procreation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the apostate's wealth is confiscated.
C) Because the apostate cannot have children.
D) Because the apostate is considered a non-Muslim and thus cannot marry a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
An apostate's marriage is invalid because the permanent status of wajib al-qatl prevents the achievement of the benefits for which marriage was legislated.

Child's Religion When Parents Differ: Kitabi versus Majusi
Hanafi ruling with Shafi'i dispute on child's religion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the child's religion when one parent is Kitabi and the other Majusi, explain the evidence based on kindness (shafaqat), and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument from ta'arud (conflict) and tarjih (preponderance).

Core Idea
Kindness to the child (shafaqat) outweighs the principle of preferring prohibition, so the child follows the better religion (Kitabi).

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario: when neither parent is Muslim, but one is a Kitabi (Jew or Christian) and the other is a Majusi (Zoroastrian), which religion does the minor child follow? The Hanafi school, guided by the principle of kindness to the child (shafaqat), rules that the child follows the Kitabi parent. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, contrasts it with the Shafi'i position, and highlights the practical consequences of the difference.

Guided Path
1
State the agreed-upon rule when one parent is Muslim
Identify the ruling that if one spouse is Muslim, the child is Muslim. This is unanimous among the four schools. Note the condition that both must be in the same dar (territory).
This provides the baseline for understanding the dispute case.
2
Identify the disputed scenario: Kitabi vs Majusi
Recognize that when one parent is Kitabi and the other Majusi, there is ikhtilaf. State the Hanafi position: the child is Kitabi.
The child is considered Kitabi for all legal purposes (e.g., slaughter, marriage).
3
Analyze the Hanafi dalil: shafaqat (kindness)
Understand that the Hanafi proof is kindness to the child: Kitabi is better than Majusi in this world (lawful slaughter, valid marriage with Muslims) and the Hereafter (lesser punishment). This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) based on maslaha.
Shafaqat overrides the general rule of preferring prohibition when there is conflict.
4
Examine the Shafi'i objection and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that there is a conflict (ta'arud) between two possible attachments. If the child follows the Majusi, the result is prohibition; if follows the Kitabi, it is permissibility. The Shafi'i rule prefers prohibition as a precaution, so the child is Majusi.
This is a methodological difference: Shafi'i usul gives precedence to prohibition in cases of doubt.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and the tarjih
Al-Marghinani states that preponderance (tarjih) is given to kindness to the child. The child's welfare overrides the abstract preference for prohibition. Thus, the child is Kitabi.
The practical consequence: under Hanafi ruling, the child's slaughter is halal and marriage to a Muslim is valid; under Shafi'i, it is not.

زوجین میں سے ایک مسلمان ہو تو بچہ مسلمان ہوگا فان كان احد الزوجين مسلما فالولد علی دینه و کذالک ان اسلم احدهما وله ولدصغير صارولده مسلما با سلامه لان فى جعله تبعاله نظراله ولو كان احدهما كتابيا والأخر مجوسيافالولد کتابی لان فيه نوع نظر له اذا المجوسية شرمنه و الشافعي يخالفنا فيه للتعارض ونحن اثبتنا الترجيح ترجمہ . پس اگر احد الزوجین مسلمان ہے تو بچہ اسی کے دین پر ہوگا۔ اور ایسے ہی اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک مسلمان ہو گیا اور حال یہ کہ اس کیلئے صغیر بچہ ہے تو اس کا بچہ احد الزوجین کے اسلام کی وجہ سے مسلمان ہو گا۔ اسلئے کہ بچہ کو مسلمان کے تابع بنانے میں بچہ کیلئے نظر شفقت ہے۔ اور اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک کتابی اور دوسرا مجوسی ہے تو بچہ کتابی ہو گا۔ کیونکہ اس میں بچہ کیلئے ایک قسم کی شفقت ہے۔ اس وجہ سے کہ مجوسی ہونا کتابی ہونے سے بدتر ہے اور امام شافعی بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنانے میں ہمارے مخالف ہیں تعارض کی وجہ سے اور ہم نے ترجیح ثابت کر دی۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر احد الزوجین مسلمان ہو تو بچہ دین اسلام پر ہوگا۔ اس پر ائمہ اربعہ کا اجماع ہے۔ اسکی صورت یہ ہے کہ زوجین کا فر ہیں۔ پھر عورت مسلمان ہو گئی یا مرد مسلمان ہو گیا۔ اسکے بعد آخر پر اسلام پیش کرنے سے پہلے بچہ پیدا ہو گیا تو اب یہ بچہ دین میں مسلمان کے تابع ہوگا۔ بشرطیکہ بچہ اور احد الزوجین میں جو مسلمان ہے دونوں ایک دار میں ہوں۔ اور اگر تباین دارین ہو گیا بائیں طور کہ مسلمان باپ دار الاسلام میں ہے اور بچہ دار الحرب میں یا اس کے برعکس ہے تو بچہ اپنے باپ کے اسلام کی وجہ سے مسلمان نہیں ہے گا۔ دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ احد الزوجین مسلمان ہو گیا اور اس کا ایک صغیر بچہ ہے تو یہ بچہ احد الزوجین کے اسلام کی وجہ سے مسلمان ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس بچہ کو مسلمان کے تابع بنانے میں اس پر شفقت ہے دنیا میں بھی اور آخرت میں بھی دنیا میں تو یہ کہ اس کے ساتھ کفار جیسا سلوک نہیں کیا جائے گا اور مرنے کے بعد مسلمانوں کے قبرستان میں دفن کریں گے۔ اور آخرت میں یہ کہ عذاب جہنم سے نجات پائے گا۔ اور اگر احد الزوجین کتابی اور دوسرا مجوی نیابت پرست ہے تو بچہ کتابی کے تابع ہوگا۔ حتی کہ اس کا ذبیحہ حلال اور مسلمان کے ساتھ اس کا نکاح جائز ہو گا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنانے میں بھی بچہ پر ایک گونہ شفقت ہے۔ اسلئے کہ مجوسی ہونا کتابی ہونے سے زیادہ بدتر ہے چنانچہ دنیا میں کتابی کا ذبیحہ حلال اور کتابیہ سے مسلمان کا نکاح درست ہے اور آخرت میں اہل کتاب کا عذاب مجوس سے کم ہو گا۔ حضرت امام شافعی بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنانے میں ہمارے مخالف ہیں۔ چنانچہ اگر باپ کتابی اور ماں مجوسی ہے تو امام شافعی کا اصل قول یہ ہے کہ بچہ جوی ہو گا۔ اس کے قائل امام احمد ہیں۔ اور دوسرا قول یہ ہے کہ بچہ باپ کے تابع ہو کر کتابی ہو گا۔ اس کے قائل امام مالک ہیں۔ اور اگر ماں کتابی اور باپ مجوسی ہے تو امام شافعی کا صرف ایک قول ہے کہ بچہ باپ کے تابع ہو کر مجوسی ہوگا نہ اس کا ذبیحہ حلال ہوگا اور نہ اس کے ساتھ کسی مسلمان کا نکاح درست ہو گا۔ لتعارض سے امام شافعی کی دلیل ہے۔ دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ الحاقین میں تعارض ہے یعنی اگر بچہ کو مجوسی کے ساتھ لاحق کریں تو اس کے ساتھ مسلمان کا نکاح اور اس کا ذبیحہ حرام ہوگا۔ اور اگر کتابی کے ساتھ الحاق کریں تو اس کا ذبیحہ بھی حلال اور اس کے ساتھ مسلمان کا نکاح بھی جائز ہوگا۔ حاصل یہ کہ مجوسی کے ساتھ الحاق موجب حرمت ہے اور کتابی کے ساتھ موجب حلت ہے اور قاعدہ ہے کہ جب حرمت اور حلت جمع ہو جائیں تو ترجیح حرمت کو ہو گی۔ اور حرمت مجوسی کے تابع بنانے میں ہے۔ اسلئے بچہ کو مجوسی کے تابع کیا گیا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ہم نے وجہ ترجیح بیان کر دی۔ وہ یہ کہ بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنانے میں اس پر شفقت ہے۔ اسلئے بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنایا گیا ہے۔
If one of the spouses is Muslim, then the child follows his religion. Similarly, if one of them becomes Muslim and he has a minor child, his child becomes Muslim by his Islam, because making the child follow him is kindness to the child. And if one of them is a Kitabi (Scriptuary) and the other a Majusi (fire-worshipper), then the child is Kitabi, because there is a kind of kindness to the child in that, since being Majusi is worse than being Kitabi. Al-Shafi'i opposes us in this due to conflict (ta'arud), and we have established preponderance (tarjih). Translation: So if one of the spouses is Muslim, the child will be on his religion. Similarly, if one of them becomes Muslim and he has a minor child, then his child becomes Muslim due to his Islam, because making the child follow him is kindness to the child. And if one of them is Kitabi and the other Majusi, then the child will be Kitabi, because there is kindness to the child in that, since being Majusi is worse than being Kitabi. Imam Shafi'i opposes us in making the child follow the Kitabi due to conflict, and we have established preponderance. Explanation: If one spouse is Muslim, the child becomes Muslim – there is consensus of the four imams. The scenario: both spouses are disbelievers, then the woman or man becomes Muslim, and the child is born before the other accepts Islam; then the child follows the Muslim, provided both are in the same dar (territory). If they are in different dar (e.g., Muslim father in dar al-Islam, child in dar al-harb), the child does not become Muslim. Second scenario: one spouse becomes Muslim and has a minor child, then the child becomes Muslim due to that. The proof: there is kindness in this world and the Hereafter. If one is Kitabi and the other Majusi, the child is Kitabi, so lawful is his slaughtered meat and lawful is a Muslim's marriage to him/her. The proof is kindness, because being Majusi is worse. Imam Shafi'i opposes: if father Kitabi and mother Majusi, his original view is that the child is Majusi (also held by Ahmad), and a second view that the child is Kitabi (held by Malik). If mother Kitabi and father Majusi, Shafi'i has only one view: the child is Majusi, so his slaughter is not lawful nor Muslim marriage. His proof: conflict between two attachments; following Majusi leads to prohibition, following Kitabi leads to permissibility; the rule is that when prohibition and permissibility conflict, preference is given to prohibition, so the child is Majusi. The author of al-Hidayah says we have stated the reason for preponderance: it is kindness to the child to make him follow the Kitabi, so we prefer that.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the religion of a child when parents are of different religions, specifically when one parent is Kitabi (Jewish or Christian) and the other is Majusi (Zoroastrian). The Hanafi position is clear: if one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim, and there is consensus on this. If both are non-Muslim but one is Kitabi and the other Majusi, the child follows the Kitabi. The reason is kindness (shafaqat) to the child, since Kitabi is better than Majusi both in this world (e.g., slaughter is lawful, Muslim marriage is valid) and the Hereafter (lesser punishment). Imam al-Shafi'i, according to the commentary, uses a different principle: when there is a conflict (ta'arud) between two possible attachments, and one leads to prohibition (if the child follows the Majusi, his slaughter and marriage are forbidden) and the other leads to permissibility (if he follows the Kitabi), the rule in his school is to prefer prohibition as a precaution. Therefore, he rules that the child is Majusi, following the worse parent. The Hanafi commentator (author of al-Hidayah) responds by asserting that preponderance (tarjih) is given to kindness to the child, which is the overriding consideration. This is a classic example of Hanafi reliance on maslaha (public interest) and istihsan (juristic preference) over strict analogical reasoning. The passage also notes that if one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim only if both are in the same dar (territory); otherwise, territorial difference prevents the child's Islam. The commentary mentions that Imam Ahmad holds a view similar to Shafi'i's original, while Imam Malik holds the Hanafi view in this case.

Key Terms
مجوسی (majusi): A follower of Zoroastrianism, considered a non-scriptuary polytheist by Hanafis.
کتابی (kitabi): A person who follows a revealed scripture (Jew or Christian); their slaughter is halal, and Muslim men may marry their women.
شفقت (shafaqat): Kindness, mercy, benevolent consideration for the child's best interest.
تعارض (ta'arud): Conflict or contradiction between two proofs or principles; here between attaching the child to the Kitabi or the Majusi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the mother is a Kitabiyya (Jewish) and the father is a Majusi (Zoroastrian), what is the religion of their child?
A) The child follows the father and is Majusi
B) The child follows the mother and is Kitabi
C) The child is Muslim because Islam is the true religion
D) The child is free to choose his own religion when he reaches puberty

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes the child's welfare (shafaqat) over abstract legal principles, ruling that a child of a Kitabi and a Majusi parent follows the Kitabi.

Religion of a Child When Parents Differ in Faith
Hanafi ruling with Shafi'i opposition on child's religion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for determining a child's religion when parents have different faiths, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of compassion (shafaqa).

Core Idea
Compassion over principle: child follows the religion that benefits him most, even against maxim that prohibition prevails.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a delicate fiqh issue: the religion of a child born to parents of different faiths. The Hanafi school grounds its ruling on compassion (shafaqa) for the child, preferring the religion that benefits him most in this world and the Hereafter. You will see how the Hanafi approach differs from Imam Shafi'i's, who applies the legal maxim that when permissibility and prohibition conflict, prohibition takes precedence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling for two scenarios: one parent Muslim, or one Kitabi and the other Majusi. The child follows the parent with the better religion.
This is based on compassion (shafaqa) for the child.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Shafi'i opposes the Hanafi ruling for the non-Muslim case; he says the child follows the Majusi when one parent is Kitabi and the other Majusi, due to the rule of preferring prohibition.
Shafi'i also has variations depending on which parent is which.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand Shafi'i's dalil: when halal and haram conflict, haram takes precedence; attributing to Majusi imposes prohibitions (slaughter, marriage), so that should be chosen.
This is a standard usul principle.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rebuts that compassion (shafaqa) overrides the general principle; the child benefits more from being Kitabi (lesser punishment, halal dealings).
The author of Al-Hidayah confirms this tarjih.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical difference: Hanafi: child's slaughter halal, marriage to Muslim valid. Shafi'i: both are invalid.
This shows the real-world impact of the ikhtilaf.

ہو گا۔ اسلئے کہ بچہ کو مسلمان کے تابع بنانے میں بچہ کیلئے نظر شفقت ہے۔ اور اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک کتابی اور دوسرا مجوسی ہے تو بچہ کتابی ہو گا۔ کیونکہ اس میں بچہ کیلئے ایک قسم کی شفقت ہے۔ اس وجہ سے کہ مجوسی ہونا کتابی ہونے سے بدتر ہے اور امام شافعی بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنانے میں ہمارے مخالف ہیں تعارض کی وجہ سے اور ہم نے ترجیح ثابت کر دی۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر احد الزوجین مسلمان ہو تو بچہ دین اسلام پر ہوگا۔ اس پر ائمہ اربعہ کا اجماع ہے۔ اسکی صورت یہ ہے کہ زوجین کا فر ہیں۔ پھر عورت مسلمان ہو گئی یا مرد مسلمان ہو گیا۔ اسکے بعد آخر پر اسلام پیش کرنے سے پہلے بچہ پیدا ہو گیا تو اب یہ بچہ دین میں مسلمان کے تابع ہوگا۔ بشرطیکہ بچہ اور احد الزوجین میں جو مسلمان ہے دونوں ایک دار میں ہوں۔ اور اگر تباین دارین ہو گیا بائیں طور کہ مسلمان باپ دار الاسلام میں ہے اور بچہ دار الحرب میں یا اس کے برعکس ہے تو بچہ اپنے باپ کے اسلام کی وجہ سے مسلمان نہیں ہے گا۔ دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ احد الزوجین مسلمان ہو گیا اور اس کا ایک صغیر بچہ ہے تو یہ بچہ احد الزوجین کے اسلام کی وجہ سے مسلمان ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس بچہ کو مسلمان کے تابع بنانے میں اس پر شفقت ہے دنیا میں بھی اور آخرت میں بھی دنیا میں تو یہ کہ اس کے ساتھ کفار جیسا سلوک نہیں کیا جائے گا اور مرنے کے بعد مسلمانوں کے قبرستان میں دفن کریں گے۔ اور آخرت میں یہ کہ عذاب جہنم سے نجات پائے گا۔ اور اگر احد الزوجین کتابی اور دوسرا مجوی نیابت پرست ہے تو بچہ کتابی کے تابع ہوگا۔ حتی کہ اس کا ذبیحہ حلال اور مسلمان کے ساتھ اس کا نکاح جائز ہو گا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنانے میں بھی بچہ پر ایک گونہ شفقت ہے۔ اسلئے کہ مجوسی ہونا کتابی ہونے سے زیادہ بدتر ہے چنانچہ دنیا میں کتابی کا ذبیحہ حلال اور کتابیہ سے مسلمان کا نکاح درست ہے اور آخرت میں اہل کتاب کا عذاب مجوس سے کم ہو گا۔ حضرت امام شافعی بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنانے میں ہمارے مخالف ہیں۔ چنانچہ اگر باپ کتابی اور ماں مجوسی ہے تو امام شافعی کا اصل قول یہ ہے کہ بچہ جوی ہو گا۔ اس کے قائل امام احمد ہیں۔ اور دوسرا قول یہ ہے کہ بچہ باپ کے تابع ہو کر کتابی ہو گا۔ اس کے قائل امام مالک ہیں۔ اور اگر ماں کتابی اور باپ مجوسی ہے تو امام شافعی کا صرف ایک قول ہے کہ بچہ باپ کے تابع ہو کر مجوسی ہوگا نہ اس کا ذبیحہ حلال ہوگا اور نہ اس کے ساتھ کسی مسلمان کا نکاح درست ہو گا۔ لتعارض سے امام شافعی کی دلیل ہے۔ دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ الحاقین میں تعارض ہے یعنی اگر بچہ کو مجوسی کے ساتھ لاحق کریں تو اس کے ساتھ مسلمان کا نکاح اور اس کا ذبیحہ حرام ہوگا۔ اور اگر کتابی کے ساتھ الحاق کریں تو اس کا ذبیحہ بھی حلال اور اس کے ساتھ مسلمان کا نکاح بھی جائز ہوگا۔ حاصل یہ کہ مجوسی کے ساتھ الحاق موجب حرمت ہے اور کتابی کے ساتھ موجب حلت ہے اور قاعدہ ہے کہ جب حرمت اور حلت جمع ہو جائیں تو ترجیح حرمت کو ہو گی۔ اور حرمت مجوسی کے تابع بنانے میں ہے۔ اسلئے بچہ کو مجوسی کے تابع کیا گیا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ہم نے وجہ ترجیح بیان کر دی۔ وہ یہ کہ بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنانے میں اس پر شفقت ہے۔ اسلئے بچہ کو کتابی کے تابع بنایا گیا ہے۔
Translation: So if one of the spouses is a Muslim, the child will be upon his religion. Likewise, if one of them becomes Muslim and he has a minor child, then his child becomes Muslim due to the Islam of one of the spouses, because making the child follow the Muslim is an act of compassion for the child. And if one of them is a Kitabi (People of the Book) and the other is a Majusi (Zoroastrian), then the child will be a Kitabi, because in that there is a kind of compassion for the child, since being a Majusi is worse than being a Kitabi. Imam Shafi'i opposes us in making the child follow the Kitabi due to contradiction, and we have established the preference. Explanation: The scenario is that if one of the spouses is Muslim, the child will be upon the religion of Islam. There is consensus of the four Imams on this. Its case is that the spouses are disbelievers. Then the woman becomes Muslim or the man becomes Muslim. After that, before the other one presents Islam, a child is born, then this child will follow the Muslim in religion, provided that the child and the Muslim spouse are in the same abode. If there is difference of abodes, such that the Muslim father is in Dar al-Islam and the child is in Dar al-Harb, or vice versa, then the child will not be Muslim due to the father's Islam. The second case is that one of the spouses becomes Muslim and he has a minor child, then this child will be Muslim due to the Islam of that spouse. The proof is that making the child follow the Muslim is compassion for him in both worlds: in this world, he will not be treated like the disbelievers, and after death he will be buried in the Muslim cemetery; in the Hereafter, he will be saved from the punishment of Hell. And if one of the spouses is Kitabi and the other is a Majusi, the child will follow the Kitabi, even to the extent that his slaughtered animal is halal and his marriage to a Muslim is permissible. The proof is that making the child follow the Kitabi is also a kind of compassion for the child, because being a Majusi is worse than being a Kitabi. Thus, in this world, the Kitabi's slaughter is halal and marriage with a Kitabi woman is permissible for a Muslim, and in the Hereafter, the punishment of the People of the Book will be less than that of the Majus. Imam Shafi'i opposes us in making the child follow the Kitabi. So, if the father is Kitabi and the mother is Majusi, Imam Shafi'i's principal view is that the child will be a Majusi – this is also the view of Imam Ahmad. His second view is that the child will follow the father and be a Kitabi – this is the view of Imam Malik. And if the mother is Kitabi and the father is Majusi, Imam Shafi'i has only one view: the child will follow the father and be a Majusi, so neither his slaughter is halal nor is his marriage to a Muslim valid. Due to contradiction, this is Imam Shafi'i's proof. The gist of the proof is that there is contradiction between the two attributions: if we attribute the child to the Majusi, then marriage to a Muslim and his slaughter become prohibited; and if we attribute him to the Kitabi, then his slaughter becomes halal and marriage to a Muslim becomes permissible. In short, attribution to the Majusi necessitates prohibition, and attribution to the Kitabi necessitates permissibility, and the rule is that when prohibition and permissibility coincide, prohibition is given preference. And prohibition lies in making him follow the Majusi, so the child is made to follow the Majusi. The author of Al-Hidayah says: We have stated the reason for preference, which is that making the child follow the Kitabi involves compassion for the child. Therefore, the child is made to follow the Kitabi.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi ruling on the religion of a child when the parents differ in faith. The passage begins by stating the general principle: if one parent is Muslim, the child follows the Muslim parent. This is based on compassion (shafaqa) for the child, as Islam is the best religion. The text then explains the consensus of the four Imams on this point. It details two scenarios: first, when both parents were originally non-Muslim and one converts before the child is born; second, when one parent converts and there is already a minor child. In both cases, the child becomes Muslim if both are in the same abode (dar). If they are in different abodes (e.g., Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb), the rule does not apply. The proof given is that following the Muslim benefits the child both in this world (proper treatment and burial) and the Hereafter (salvation from Hell). Next, the passage discusses the case where one parent is a Kitabi (Jew or Christian) and the other a Majusi (Zoroastrian). The Hanafi position is that the child follows the Kitabi, because that is the less harmful option – being a Kitabi is closer to Islam than being a Majusi. The practical consequences are that the child's slaughtered meat is halal and marriage to a Muslim is valid. The text then presents the Shafi'i opposition. Imam Shafi'i argues that due to conflicting claims, the child should follow the Majusi because when prohibition (haram) and permissibility (halal) conflict, prohibition takes precedence as a legal maxim. The Hanafi response, as stated by the author of Al-Hidayah, is that the preference for the Kitabi is based on compassion for the child, overriding the general maxim. Thus, the Hanafi school chooses the more merciful outcome.

Key Terms
تابع (tābiʿ): The child is deemed to follow the religion of the parent with superior faith.
شفقت (shafaqa): Compassion and mercy shown to the child by giving him the better religion.
الحاق (ilhāq): Attributing the child to one parent's religion.
تعارض (taʿāruḍ): Conflict between two proofs or considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When one parent is Kitabi and the other is Majusi, according to Hanafi fiqh, what is the child's religion?
A) The child is Majusi.
B) The child is Kitabi.
C) The child chooses upon reaching puberty.
D) The child follows the father regardless.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives the child the religion that is most beneficial, using compassion (shafaqa) as the overriding principle, even against the general maxim that prohibition takes precedence.

Nikah — Marriage

Presenting Islam to the Non-Muslim Spouse: Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Hanafi ruling on qadi's presentation and nature of separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on presenting Islam to a non-Muslim spouse and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the qadi's separation constitutes a divorce (talaq).

Core Idea
The qadi must present Islam; separation is talaq only when the wife converts.

When one spouse converts to Islam, the marriage's foundation is shaken. The Hanafi school mandates that the qadi formally invite the non-Muslim spouse to embrace Islam. If they refuse, the qadi separates them. But what is the legal nature of that separation? Is it a divorce or a mere dissolution? This lesson unpacks the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine who converts first: wife or husband. The ruling on the nature of separation differs based on which spouse converts. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that when the wife converts and the husband refuses, the separation is talaq; when the husband converts and the wife refuses, it is not talaq.
The text explicitly differentiates these two cases.
2
Grasp the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf disagrees, stating that in both cases the separation is not talaq because the cause (conversion and refusal) is shared by both spouses. Understand his rationale: like separation due to ownership (milk), where both parties contribute.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority within the school.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that presenting Islam harasses dhimmis, violating the dhimma contract. Instead, the marriage ends immediately if before consummation, or after a waiting period if after. The Hanafi response: the qadi merely offers, does not force; without presentation, there is no clear cause for separation.
The Hanafi proof (dalil aqli) focuses on the loss of marriage objectives and the need for a definitive cause.
4
Examine the qiyas with the eunuch/impotent man
The text likens the husband's refusal to a physical defect that prevents fulfillment of marriage objectives. Just as the qadi can annul due to impotence, here the qadi substitutes for the husband in 'releasing with kindness' (tasrih bi al-ihsan) when the husband fails to retain with kindness.
This analogy supports the view that separation is talaq (since the qadi acts as the husband's proxy in divorce).
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: presenting Islam is wajib, and separation is talaq only when the wife converts. Abu Yusuf's view is considered weak (marjuh).
Al-Hidayah favors this view.

Translation: And when a woman becomes Muslim while her husband is a disbeliever, the qadi will present Islam to her husband. If he becomes Muslim, then she is his wife; if he refuses, the qadi will separate between them, and this separation is a divorce (talaq) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And if a husband becomes Muslim while he has a Majusi wife, he presents Islam to her; if she becomes Muslim, she is his wife; if she refuses, the qadi separates between them, and this separation is not a divorce. Abu Yusuf said the separation is not a divorce in both cases. As for presenting Islam, that is our madhhab. Imam Shafi'i said that Islam should not be presented, because presenting Islam involves harassing the dhimmis, whereas by the contract of dhimma we have guaranteed that we will not harass them. However, the marital bond before consummation is not strong, so it is severed by the very act of Islam; after consummation it is strong, so a respite of three menstrual periods is given, as in divorce. Our proof is that the objectives of marriage are lost, so there must be a cause on which the separation is based, and embracing Islam is an act of obedience and cannot be a cause for separation. So Islam is presented so that either the objectives are achieved through Islam, or separation is established through refusal. The proof of Abu Yusuf's view is that the separation occurs due to a cause in which both spouses participate, so it is not a divorce, like separation due to ownership. And the proof of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that by refusing, the husband refrained from retention with kindness despite being able to do so by embracing Islam, so the qadi takes his place in releasing with kindness, as in the case of a eunuch or impotent man.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage discusses the procedure when one spouse converts to Islam and the other remains a non-Muslim. It presents the Hanafi position that the qadi must present Islam to the non-Muslim spouse. If the spouse converts, the marriage continues; if not, the qadi separates them. The nature of this separation is disputed within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider it a divorce (talaq) when the wife converts and the husband refuses, but not when the husband converts and the wife refuses (she is a woman, and divorce is the husband's right). Abu Yusuf holds that in both cases it is not a divorce because the cause (conversion and refusal) is shared by both spouses. The text then presents the Shafi'i objection: Shafi'i argues that present Islam is interference with dhimmis, contradicting the dhimma contract. Instead, Shafi'i says if the conversion occurs before consummation, the marriage ends immediately; if after, there is a waiting period like divorce. The Hanafi response is that the objectives of marriage have failed, so a clear cause for separation is needed; conversion itself is obedience, not a ground for separation. The qadi's presentation is a means to achieve the goals or to establish refusal. The passage ends by comparing the husband's refusal to the case of a man with a physical defect (like impotence), where the qadi can separate. The ruling is a key example of how the Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to balance compassion with legal consistency.

Key Terms
عرض اسلام (ʿarḍ al-islām): The qadi's formal invitation to the non-Muslim spouse to embrace Islam.
فرقت (furqa): The separation ordered by the qadi upon the spouse's refusal to convert.
امساک بالمعروف (imsāk bi al-maʿrūf): Retention of the wife in marriage with kindness, as commanded in the Qur'an.
تسریح بالاحسان (tasrīḥ bi al-iḥsān): Releasing the wife with kindness, i.e., divorce or separation in a good manner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is a dissolution of marriage without divorce.
B) It is considered a divorce (talaq).
C) It is an annulment (faskh) based on lack of consent.
D) The marriage continues until the husband embraces Islam later.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse is wajib, and the qadi's separation is considered talaq only when the wife converts and the husband refuses.

Nikah — Marriage

Presenting Islam to the Non-Muslim Spouse After Conversion
Inter-madhhab dispute on presenting Islam and nature of separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse and distinguish between the rulings when the husband or wife refuses, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The judge must present Islam; separation is talaq if the husband refuses, faskh if the wife refuses.

When one spouse converts to Islam, the marriage faces a new religious reality. The judge must offer Islam to the remaining non-Muslim spouse. But if they refuse, what kind of separation occurs? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad distinguish based on who refuses; Abu Yusuf disagrees; and al-Shafi'i questions the presentation itself. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, wife converts, husband is non-Muslim. Second, husband converts, wife is Majusi (Zoroastrian).
The text specifically mentions Majusi wife; the same ruling applies to other non-Muslims according to the madhhab.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the nature of separation
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: separation is talaq when the husband refuses, faskh when the wife refuses. Abu Yusuf: both cases are faskh.
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad as presented in the text.
3
Understand the Hanafi dalil for presenting Islam
The objectives of marriage (companionship, procreation, mutual support) are lost due to religious difference; presenting Islam provides a clear legal basis either for continuation (conversion) or separation (refusal).
Hanafis argue that the need for legal clarity overrides the general prohibition on interfering with dhimmis.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) used
The judge's role in separation is analogized to the case of a castrated or impotent husband: the judge substitutes for the husband in effecting a release (tasrih bi ihsan).
This analogy supports treating the separation as talaq when the husband refuses, because the judge acts in place of the husband.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In the Hanafi school, the non-Muslim spouse is given a chance to convert; the separation counts as talaq (reducing the husband's available divorces) or faskh (no effect on divorce count) depending on the scenario. In the Shafi'i school, marriage ends immediately upon conversion without presentation.
This difference affects the iddah period and mahr entitlements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And when a woman becomes Muslim while her husband is a disbeliever, the judge presents Islam to him. If he becomes Muslim, she is his wife. If he refuses, the judge separates them, and that is a divorce according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And if a man becomes Muslim while his wife is a Zoroastrian, the judge presents Islam to her. If she becomes Muslim, she is his wife. If she refuses, the judge separates them, and the separation is not a divorce. Abu Yusuf said: The separation is not a divorce in either case. As for presenting Islam, that is our madhhab. al-Shafi'i said: Islam should not be presented, because that would be molesting them, and we have guaranteed in the treaty of dhimma not to molest them. However, the ownership of marriage before consummation is not confirmed, so it is terminated upon Islam itself; after consummation it is confirmed, so it is deferred until the completion of three menstruations, as in divorce. Our proof is that the objectives have been lost, so there must be a basis for separation. Islam is obedience and cannot be a cause for its loss, so Islam is presented so that the objectives may be achieved through Islam or separation established through refusal. The basis of Abu Yusuf's view is that the separation occurs due to a cause shared by both spouses, so it is not a divorce, like separation due to ownership. Their proof is that by refusing Islam, the husband refrained from keeping her with kindness while able to do so by becoming Muslim, so the judge takes his place in releasing with kindness, as in the case of one who is castrated or impotent. As for the wife, she is not eligible for divorce, so the judge does not take her place upon her refusal. When the judge separates them due to her refusal, she gets the mahr if he had consummated, because it is confirmed by consummation. If he had not consummated, she has no mahr, because the separation came from her side and the mahr was not confirmed, so it resembles apostasy and mutual consent.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah discusses the ruling when one spouse becomes Muslim and the other remains non-Muslim. The judge must present Islam to the non-Muslim spouse. If the husband converts (when the wife already Muslim), the marriage continues. If he refuses, the judge separates them, and according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, this separation counts as a divorce (talaq). In the reverse case (husband Muslim, wife Majusi), if she refuses, the separation is not a divorce but a faskh (annulment). Abu Yusuf holds that both cases are faskh, not talaq. The Shafi'i school argues that presenting Islam to a dhimmi violates the protection treaty, and the marriage should automatically terminate upon conversion without waiting. The Hanafi response is that the objectives of marriage are lost and need a clear cause for separation; presenting Islam either achieves the objective or establishes separation. The analogy to the cases of jabb (castration) and 'innah (impotence) supports the judge's role in effecting separation. Finally, the mahr consequences: if consummated, full mahr; if not, no mahr because the wife's refusal caused the separation.

Key Terms
عرض الإسلام (arḍ al-islām): The judge formally offers Islam to the non-Muslim spouse before effecting separation.
فرقة (furqa): Judicial separation between spouses due to difference in religion.
جب وعنة (jabb wa 'innah): Castration and impotence, used as analogy for the husband's inability to keep the wife with kindness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Talaq (divorce), counting towards the three divorces

D) A conditional divorce that becomes effective after iddah

Key Takeaway
The judge must present Islam to the non-Muslim spouse; separation is talaq if the husband refuses, faskh if the wife refuses, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Nikah — Marriage

Presenting Islam to the Non-Muslim Spouse (Inter-Madhhab Dispute)
Inter-madhhab dispute on presentation of Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi obligation to present Islam to a non-Muslim spouse, evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse is wajib; the refusal becomes the cause for tafriq.

This lesson focuses on the Hanafi requirement that a qadi must present Islam to the non-Muslim spouse before any separation due to religious difference. This is a point of disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that such presentation violates the dhimma contract. We will examine the proofs on both sides and understand the practical difference in rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the Hanafi position
Identify the disputed question: should the qadi present Islam to the non-Muslim spouse before separation? The Hanafi school says yes – it is wajib. This is the mufta bihi position.
The passage explicitly states 'آخر پر اسلام پیش کرنا ہمارا مذہب ہے' (presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse is our madhhab).
2
Identify the opposing school and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i objects: presenting Islam amounts to interference with dhimmis, which violates the contract of dhimma. He also argues that the marriage dissolves automatically upon one spouse's conversion, without need for presentation.
The Shafi'i proof is based on the guarantee of non-interference in the dhimma contract.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis distinguish between compulsory interference (which is prohibited) and voluntary invitation (which is not). They also provide rational proof: the objectives of marriage are lost due to the spouse's persistence in disbelief, but the original disbelief did not prevent the marriage – so a new cause (the refusal upon presentation) is needed to justify separation.
The Hanafi proof also includes a hadith from the Muwatta about Safwan, and actions of Umar (RA).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis require active presentation and the separation is delayed until after refusal. Shafi'is hold that no presentation is needed and separation occurs immediately upon conversion (or after waiting period if consummated).
This affects the timeline of the separation and the possibility of reconciliation.
5
Evaluate the authority and tarjih
The Hanafi position is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi. Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah prefers it. It is supported by hadith and the practice of the Sahaba.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih – a legitimate disagreement.
